فإن قسم وسلّمَ صح، فإن وهب دقيقاً في بر، أو دهناً في سمسم لا، وإن طحن، أو أخرج وسلم، وكذا السمن في اللبن (فإن قُسِمَ وسُلَّمَ) صح) : أي إذا وهب النصف المشاع، ثم قُسِمَ وسُلّمَ صح؛ لأن تمامها بالقبض عندنا، وعند القبض لا شيوع، فإن وهب دقيقاً في بر، أو دهناً في سمسم لا، وإن طحن أو أخرج وسلّم، وكذا السمن في اللبن، إنما لا يجوز لأن الموهوب معدوم وقت الهبة. [قوله: فإن قسم وسلّم؛ ولو سلّمه شائعاً لا يملكه، حتى لا ينفذ تصرفه فيه، ويكون مضموناً عليه، وينفذ فيه تصرُف الواهب. ذكره الطحاوي وقاضي خان.] [قوله: وعند القبض لا شيوع؛ فالمعتبر هو الشيوع وقت القبض لا وقت العقد، حتى لو وهب نصف الدار مشاعاً ولم يُسَلّم، ثم وهب النصف الآخر وسلّم الكل جاز. كذا في «العناية» وغيرها.] [قوله: لأن الموهوب معدوم وقت الهبة؛ لأنه ليس بموجود بالفعل، وإنما يحدث بالعصر والطحن، وكونه موجوداً بالقوة غير معتبر؛ لأن عامة الممكنات كذلك، ولا تسمى موجودة.]
If he divided and delivered, it is valid. And if he gifted flour in wheat, or oil in sesame, it is not valid, even if he grinds or extracts and delivers. Similarly for clarified butter in milk. (If divided and delivered, it is valid): meaning if he gifted the undivided half, then divided and delivered, it is valid, because its completion is by taking possession in our school, and at the time of taking possession there is no undividedness. If he gifted flour in wheat, or oil in sesame, it is not valid, even if he grinds or extracts and delivers; similarly clarified butter in milk, it is not permissible because the gifted item is non-existent at the time of the gift. [His saying: If he divided and delivered; and if he delivered it undivided, he does not own it, so that his disposition in it is not effective, and it is guaranteed against him, and the donor's disposition in it is effective. Mentioned by al-Tahawi and Qadi Khan.] [His saying: And at the time of taking possession there is no undividedness; so what is considered is undividedness at the time of taking possession, not at the time of contract. Even if he gifted half the house undivided and did not deliver, then gifted the other half and delivered the whole, it is permissible. Thus in al-‘Inayah and others.] [His saying: Because the gifted item is non-existent at the time of the gift; because it does not exist actually, but only comes into being by pressing or grinding; and its existence in potential is not considered, because most potential things are such, and they are not called existent.]
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on two scenarios: (1) If an undivided share is divided and then delivered, the gift becomes valid because the invalidating factor (shuyu') is removed at the time of possession. (2) Gifts of items that are mixed or not yet separated, like flour in wheat or oil in sesame, are invalid because the specific gifted item is not actually in existence at the time of the contract; it is only potential. The commentary further explains that if the donor delivers the undivided share without division, the donee does not own it and cannot dispose of it; the donor still has control. It reiterates that the relevant time for shuyu' is at delivery, not contract, and gives an example: gifting half a house, then later the other half, and delivering the whole together is valid because at delivery there is no undividedness. The reason for invalidity of non-existing items is that they are not actual objects at contract time.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession; for validity of gift, delivery must remove undividedness.
الموهوب معدوم (al-mawhub ma'dum): The gifted item is non-existent at contract time; gift of future or potential items is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if A gifts B 10 kg of flour that is still mixed with unground wheat, and A does not separate it, what is the ruling?
A) The gift is valid because flour will exist after grinding.
B) The gift is invalid because the flour is non-existent at the time of the contract.
C) The gift is valid if A grinds and delivers immediately.
D) The gift is valid if B is a partner.

Key Takeaway
A gift of an undivided share can become valid if the share is divided and delivered; a gift of a non-existent item is forever void.

Validity of Gifting Unseparated Produce
Ruling on gifts of attached produce with proof by analogy and istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifts of attached items and explain how istihsan resolves the initial invalidity.

Core Idea
Gifts of attached items are invalid until separated and delivered; istihsan validates them after separation.

Can you gift milk that is still in the udder or wool still on the sheep? The Hanafi school addresses this by analogy to a common share, but then applies istihsan to validate the gift once the items are separated from the donor's property and delivered. This ruling balances the requirement of possession (qabd) with practical need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and initial ruling
What is being gifted? Milk in udder, wool on back, crops in ground, dates on tree. The initial rule is that such gifts are invalid (la yajuz).
Invalidity is due to prevention of possession, not non-existence.
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
The gifted item is connected to the donor's property that is not gifted, like a common share (mushā'). This connection prevents full possession.
Analogy: similar to gifting a share of an undivided property.
3
Apply the exception by istihsan
If the donor separates the gifted item (e.g., milks, shears, harvests) and delivers it to the donee, the gift becomes valid by istihsan.
Istihsan overrides the initial analogy for practical convenience.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
This ruling is specific to Hanafi school; other schools may have different views on gifts of unseparated produce.

كتاب الهبة تعريفها وأحكامها وهبة لبن في ضرع، وصوفٍ على ظهر غنم، وزَرْع ونخل في أرض، وتمر في نخيل كالمشاع، وتم هبة ما مع الموهوب له بلا قبض جديد بخلاف المشاع ، ( وهبةُ لَبَن في ضرع، وصوف على ظهر غنم، وزَرْع ونخل في أرض، وتمر في نخيل كالمشاع) : أي لا يجوز هذه الهبات، لكن إن فصلت هذه الأشياء عن ملك الواهب، وقبض تصح. (وتم هبة ما مع الموهوب له بلا قبض جديد [۱] قوله : بخلاف المشاع ; فإنّه محل للتمليك؛ لكونه موجوداً وقت العقد ويتصوّر القبض فيه، لكن لا على سبيل الكمال، ولهذا يصح بيعه بالإجماع، والمانع فيه لمعنى حق غيره، وهو العجز عن التسليم، فإذا زال المانع انقلب جائزاً. [۲] قوله : أي لا يجوز هذه الهبات ; لا لكون هذه الأشياء معدومة كما في الصورة السابقة، بل لاتصال الموهوب بما ليس بموهوب من ملك الواهب مع إمكان الفصل، فذلك يمنع القبض كالشائع؛ ولذا إن فصلت هذه الأشياء عن ملك الواهب وقبض تصح الهبة استحساناً كما في المشاع. [۳] قوله : وتم ...الخ ; تصويره : إنَّ الشيء الموهوب إذا كان في يد الموهوب له ملكه بالهبة، ولا حاجة إلى تحديد القبض فيه بأن ينتهي إلى الموضع الذي فيه ذلك الشيء، ويمضي وقت يتمكن فيه من قبضها؛ لأنَّ القبض هو الشرط، وهو متحقق. وإطلاق العبارة يشمل ما إذا كان له الشيء الموهوب في يد الموهوب أمانة أو مضمونة، ولو وديعة ، فهبةُ الوديعة للمودع، والعارية للمستعير، والمغصوب للغاصب غير محتاجة إلى قبض جديد؛ لأنَّ الموهوب حينئذ في يد الموهوب له حقيقة ، فلا يحتاج إلى قبض آخر. والأصل فيه : إنه متى تجانس القبضان نابَ أحدهما عن الآخر؛ لأنَّ التجانس دليل التشابه، والمتشابهان ينوب كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عن الآخر، ومتى لم يتجانسا ناب الأعلى عن الأدنى دون العكس؛ لأن في الأعلى ما في الأدنى وزيادة.
The Book of Gift: its definition and rulings. And a gift of milk in an udder, wool on the back of sheep, crops and palm trees in land, and dates on palm trees is like a common share. And a gift of something already with the donee is completed without new possession, unlike a common share. (And a gift of milk in an udder, wool on the back of sheep, crops and palm trees in land, and dates on palm trees is like a common share): meaning these gifts are not valid, but if these things are separated from the donor's ownership and possession occurs, they are valid. (And a gift of something already with the donee is completed without new possession). [1] His saying: 'Unlike a common share': because the common share is a valid object for ownership as it exists at the time of the contract and possession is conceivable, though not perfectly; this is why its sale is valid by consensus. The impediment in it is due to the right of another, i.e., the inability to deliver. Once the impediment is removed, it becomes valid. [2] His saying: 'These gifts are not valid': not because these things are non-existent as in the previous case, but due to the connection of the gifted item to what is not gifted from the donor's property while separation is possible. That prevents possession like a common share. Hence if these things are separated from the donor's property and possession occurs, the gift is valid by istihsan, as in the case of the common share. [3] His saying: 'And completes...' etc.: Its illustration: if the gifted item is already in the hand of the donee, he owns it by the gift, and there is no need to specify a new possession by going to the location of the item and waiting for a time sufficient for possession, because possession is the condition and it is already realized. The general wording includes whether the gifted item is in the donee's hand as an amanah (trust) or a guarantee (damān), such as a deposit (wadiah): a gift of a deposit to the depositee, or a loan to the borrower, or a usurped item to the usurper, does not need new possession because the gifted item is actually in the donee's hand, so no separate possession is needed. The principle is: whenever two acts of possession are similar in nature, one suffices for the other, because similarity indicates resemblance, and similar things substitute for each other. When they are not similar, the higher substitutes for the lower but not vice versa, because the higher contains what is in the lower and more.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but related rulings within the book of gifts (hibah). First, it discusses the invalidity of gifting items that are still attached to or mixed with the donor's property, such as milk in the udder (still part of the animal), wool on a sheep's back, crops still in the ground (unharvested), dates still on the palm tree, and similar. These are akin to gifting a common share (mushaa) because the gifted item is not yet separated from the donor's ownership. The invalidity here is not due to non-existence (as in the earlier case of flour in wheat), but due to the connection between the gifted item and what remains the donor's property, which prevents the required possession (qabd) from being complete. However, if the donor separates these items (e.g., milks the udder, shears the wool, harvests the crop, picks the dates) and then delivers them, the gift becomes valid by istihsan (juristic preference), similar to the case of a common share after division and delivery. Second, the passage explains that if the gifted item is already in the possession of the donee at the time of the gift (for example, as a trust, loan, or even as a usurped item), no new possession is required for the gift to be valid. The donee already has actual control over the item. The commentary elaborates that this applies whether the donee holds the item as an amanah (trust) or in a daman (guarantee) capacity. For instance, if someone gifts a deposit (wadiah) to the depositary, or a borrowed item to the borrower, or a stolen item to the thief (if it becomes his by gift), no new physical delivery is needed because the item is already in the donee's hand. The underlying principle is that when two possessions are of the same nature (e.g., both are amanah or both are daman), the existing possession suffices for the new one. When they differ, the higher type (e.g., daman) includes what is in the lower (amanah), but not vice versa.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference overriding qiyas for reasons of convenience or need; used here to validate gifts after separation.
قبض (qabḍ): Possession; in this context, physical control that validates a gift.
مشاع (mushā‘): Common share; undivided co-ownership share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a gift of wool on a sheep becomes valid:
A) Immediately upon the donor's statement.
B) When the wool is shorn and delivered to the donee.
C) Only if the sheep is also gifted.
D) When the donee takes possession without separation.

Key Takeaway
Gifts of attached produce are invalid until separated and delivered; istihsan validates them after fulfillment of these conditions.

Gifts Already in the Donee's Hand: No New Possession Required
Exemption from new possession for gifts already in donee's hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when no new possession is needed for a gift and apply the principle of similarity of possession.

Core Idea
If the gifted item is already in the donee's hand, no new possession is needed; existing possession suffices.

What if you want to give something that the recipient already holds—like a deposit or borrowed item? The Hanafi school simplifies the process: no need for re-delivery, as the possession already fulfills the condition. This ruling covers various situations and is based on the principle that similar possessions substitute for each other.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
The gifted item is already in the physical control of the donee at the time of the gift contract.
Examples: deposit (wadiah), loan (ariyah), or even a usurped item (maghsūb).
2
State the rule
No new possession (qabd) is required; the gift is valid immediately.
The text says 'tammat' (completed) without new possession.
3
Understand the reason
Possession (qabd) is the condition for gift validity, and it is already realized. The donee has actual or constructive control.
The general wording includes both amanah (trust) and daman (guarantee) holdings.
4
Apply the principle of similarity
When two possessions are similar in nature (e.g., both amanah), the existing one suffices for the new. When they differ, the higher (daman) substitutes for the lower (amanah), but not vice versa.
Example: a gift of a deposit to the depositary: the depositary's existing baleful possession suffices for ownership.

The Book of Gift: its definition and rulings. And a gift of milk in an udder, wool on the back of sheep, crops and palm trees in land, and dates on palm trees is like a common share. And a gift of something already with the donee is completed without new possession, unlike a common share. (And a gift of milk in an udder, wool on the back of sheep, crops and palm trees in land, and dates on palm trees is like a common share): meaning these gifts are not valid, but if these things are separated from the donor's ownership and possession occurs, they are valid. (And a gift of something already with the donee is completed without new possession). [1] His saying: 'Unlike a common share': because the common share is a valid object for ownership as it exists at the time of the contract and possession is conceivable, though not perfectly; this is why its sale is valid by consensus. The impediment in it is due to the right of another, i.e., the inability to deliver. Once the impediment is removed, it becomes valid. [2] His saying: 'These gifts are not valid': not because these things are non-existent as in the previous case, but due to the connection of the gifted item to what is not gifted from the donor's property while separation is possible. That prevents possession like a common share. Hence if these things are separated from the donor's property and possession occurs, the gift is valid by istihsan, as in the case of the common share. [3] His saying: 'And completes...' etc.: Its illustration: if the gifted item is already in the hand of the donee, he owns it by the gift, and there is no need to specify a new possession by going to the location of the item and waiting for a time sufficient for possession, because possession is the condition and it is already realized. The general wording includes whether the gifted item is in the donee's hand as an amanah (trust) or a guarantee (damān), such as a deposit (wadiah): a gift of a deposit to the depositee, or a loan to the borrower, or a usurped item to the usurper, does not need new possession because the gifted item is actually in the donee's hand, so no separate possession is needed. The principle is: whenever two acts of possession are similar in nature, one suffices for the other, because similarity indicates resemblance, and similar things substitute for each other. When they are not similar, the higher substitutes for the lower but not vice versa, because the higher contains what is in the lower and more.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but related rulings within the book of gifts (hibah). First, it discusses the invalidity of gifting items that are still attached to or mixed with the donor's property, such as milk in the udder (still part of the animal), wool on a sheep's back, crops still in the ground (unharvested), dates still on the palm tree, and similar. These are akin to gifting a common share (mushaa) because the gifted item is not yet separated from the donor's ownership. The invalidity here is not due to non-existence (as in the earlier case of flour in wheat), but due to the connection between the gifted item and what remains the donor's property, which prevents the required possession (qabd) from being complete. However, if the donor separates these items (e.g., milks the udder, shears the wool, harvests the crop, picks the dates) and then delivers them, the gift becomes valid by istihsan (juristic preference), similar to the case of a common share after division and delivery. Second, the passage explains that if the gifted item is already in the possession of the donee at the time of the gift (for example, as a trust, loan, or even as a usurped item), no new possession is required for the gift to be valid. The donee already has actual control over the item. The commentary elaborates that this applies whether the donee holds the item as an amanah (trust) or in a daman (guarantee) capacity. For instance, if someone gifts a deposit (wadiah) to the depositary, or a borrowed item to the borrower, or a stolen item to the thief (if it becomes his by gift), no new physical delivery is needed because the item is already in the donee's hand. The underlying principle is that when two possessions are of the same nature (e.g., both are amanah or both are daman), the existing possession suffices for the new one. When they differ, the higher type (e.g., daman) includes what is in the lower (amanah), but not vice versa.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadī‘ah): Deposit: property left with someone for safekeeping as a trust.
عارية (āriyah): Loan for use; borrowing of an item for no charge.
قبض (qabḍ): Possession; physical control that validates a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a gift of a deposit to the depositary:
A) Requires the depositary to return the item and then take it back.
B) Is valid without new possession because the item is already in the depositary's hand.
C) Is invalid because the depositary is a trustee.
D) Requires a formal contract, but no possession change.

Key Takeaway
When the gifted item is already in the donee's possession, no new possession is needed; existing control suffices to complete the gift.

Gift of Attached Property: Ruling and Reasoning
Explains ruling and analogy for gifts of attached/unseparated property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school invalidates gifts of items attached to the donor's property, and state the condition under which such gifts become valid by istihsan.

Core Idea
A gift of something attached to the donor's property is invalid until separated and possessed.

When someone gifts a cow's milk while it is still in the udder, or wool still on the sheep, the Hanafi school deems the gift invalid. This ruling is not because the item is non‑existent, but because it is physically connected to other property of the donor, making proper possession impossible. The same principle applies to crops, fruit on trees, and undivided shares (musha'). However, if the donor separates the item and the donee takes possession, the gift becomes valid by the principle of istihsan (juristic preference). This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the analogy behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its examples
Read the passage and list the specific examples given: milk in the udder, wool on sheep, crops and palm trees in the land, dates on palm trees. Note that each is physically attached to something else owned by the donor.
These are all cases where the gifted thing is not yet separated from the donor's property.
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
The text clarifies that the invalidity is NOT due to non‑existence (as in a future unborn animal), but because the gifted item is connected (ittiṣāl) to what is not being gifted, and separation is possible. This connection prevents valid possession (qabḍ).
Possession is a condition for the completion of a gift in Hanafi law.
3
Recognize the analogy with musha'
The ruling compares these gifts to a gift of an undivided share (musha') in jointly owned property. In both cases, the inability to possess the exact item separately renders the gift invalid until separation and possession occur.
Musha' is invalid in Hanafi school unless the co‑owner permits possession.
4
See the istihsan exception
If the donor first separates the item (e.g., milks the cow, shears the wool, harvests the fruit) and then the donee takes possession, the gift becomes valid by istihsan. This is a departure from strict analogy (qiyas) to facilitate transactions.
Istihsan is a Hanafi method of juristic preference based on public good and ease.
5
Apply to a new example
Consider a gift of the water in a well while it is still underground. Using the same reasoning, the gift would be invalid because the water is attached to the donor's land, and possession is impossible until the water is drawn. If the donor draws the water and the donee takes it, the gift becomes valid.
The key is whether the item can be physically possessed independently at the time of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهبة لبن في ضرع، وصوفٍ على ظهر غنم، وزَرْع ونخل في أرض، وتمر في نخيل كالمشاع. [2] قوله : أي لا يجوز هذه الهبات ؛ لا لكون هذه الأشياء معدومة كما في الصورة السابقة، بل لاتصال الموهوب بما ليس بموهوب من ملك الواهب مع إمكان الفصل، فذلك يمنع القبض كالشائع ؛ ولذا إن فصلت هذه الأشياء عن ملك الواهب وقبض تصح الهبة استحساناً كما في المشاع.
And the gift of milk in the udder, and wool on the back of sheep, and crops and palm trees in the land, and dates on palm trees is like the joint share (musha'). [2] He said: meaning these gifts are not permissible; not because these things are non-existent as in the previous case, but because the gifted item is connected to what is not gifted from the property of the donor while separation is possible, so that prevents possession (qabd) like the joint share. Hence if these things are separated from the donor's property and possession occurs, the gift becomes valid by istihsan, just as in the case of musha'.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqayah with its sharh. It states that certain types of gifts are invalid because the gifted item is attached to something else in the donor's property, although separation is possible. The examples given are: milk in the udder, wool on the back of sheep, crops and palm trees in the land, and dates on palm trees. The ruling is that these are like a joint share (musha') in that they are invalid unless the gifted item is separated and then possession is taken. The reason is not that the item is non-existent (as in a case where one gifts something that does not yet exist), but because the attachment prevents valid possession. The commentator clarifies that if the donor first separates the item (e.g., milks the milk or shears the wool) and then the donee takes possession, the gift becomes valid by istihsan (juristic preference). This is analogous to the ruling on musha' where separation and possession also validate the gift.

Key Terms
مشاع (musha'): An undivided share in jointly owned property that is not physically separated.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the gifted item, a condition for the validity of the gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a gift of milk in the udder become valid?
A) Immediately upon the contract, because the milk exists potentially.
B) After one year, when the milk is naturally separated.
C) Only if the donor milks the cow and the donee takes possession of the milk.
D) It is always invalid, regardless of separation.

Key Takeaway
The gift of an item attached to the donor's other property is invalid until separated and possessed, by analogy with musha'.

Completion of Gift Without New Possession: Items Already Held by Donee and Father's Gift to Minor Child
Explains when qabd is deemed fulfilled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when a gift is completed without new possession and apply the principle of homogeneity to determine if existing possession suffices.

Core Idea
Existing possession substitutes for gift possession if homogeneous or higher; a father's possession suffices for his minor child.

In Hanafi fiqh, possession (qabd) is a condition for the completion of a gift. However, when the donee already holds the gifted item—whether as a trust (amanah) or a guaranteed (dhaman) possession—the law deems the condition fulfilled without a new act. This lesson explores the principle of homogeneity substitution and the special case of a father gifting to his minor child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that if a gifted item is already in the donee's hand, no new possession (qabd) is required. The gift is valid automatically.
This applies to all cases where the donee already physically controls the item.
2
Understand Types of Possession
Possession can be amanah (trust, non-liable) or dhaman (guaranteed, liable). Examples: deposit (amanah), loan for use (amanah), usurped item (dhaman), item on approval (dhaman).
The distinction determines whether substitution is possible.
3
Apply the Homogeneity Principle
If both existing and required possessions are of the same type (e.g., both amanah), one substitutes for the other. If the existing is dhaman (higher) and required is amanah (lower), the higher substitutes. But if existing is amanah and required is dhaman (as in sale), no substitution; new possession needed.
The 'asl' (principle) is: higher substitutes for lower, not vice versa.
4
Distinguish the Sale Case
If someone holds an amanah and its owner sells it to him, a new possession is required because the sale requires dhaman possession, which is higher than amanah.
Gift and sale have different possession requirements.
5
Apply the Father-Child Rule
When a father gifts something to his minor child, the father's own possession suffices for the child, as he is the guardian. This applies even if the item is with the father's depositee. Exception: if the father's property is mortgaged, usurped, or sold invalidly, the gift is not complete because the item is not in his effective possession.
The father's hand represents the child's hand.

ذلك الشيء، ويمضي وقت يتمكن فيه من قبضها ؛ لأنَّ القبض هو الشرط، وهو متحقق. وإطلاق العبارة يشمل ما إذا كان له الشيء الموهوب في يد الموهوب أمانة أو مضمونة، ولو وديعة ، فهبةُ الوديعة للمودع ، والعارية قبض الصغير؛ لأنه وليه ، فيشترط قبضه، ولا فرق في ذلك بين ما إذا كان في يده أو في يد مودعه ؛ لأن يد المودع كيد المالك. بخلاف ما إذا كان مال الأب مرهوناً أو مغصوباً أو مبيعاً بيعاً فاسداً لم يتم الهبة الشرط.
And the gift of what is with the donee is completed without new possession, unlike the joint share. [3] He said: 'And it is complete...' etc.; its explanation: When the gifted item is in the hand of the donee, he owns it by the gift, and there is no need to specify possession (qabd) by going to the place where the item is and waiting a time in which he could take possession, because possession is the condition and it is already realized. The generality of the expression includes when the gifted item is in the donee's hand as an amanah (trust) or a dhaman (guaranteed), even a deposit, so the gift of a deposit to the depositor, a loan to the borrower, and a usurped item to the usurper do not need new possession, because the gifted item is then truly in the hand of the donee, so he does not need another possession. The principle is: whenever two possessions are homogeneous, one substitutes for the other; because homogeneity indicates similarity, and similar things substitute for each other. When they are not homogeneous, the higher substitutes for the lower but not vice versa, because the higher contains what is in the lower and more. So if a person has an amanah like a deposit and its owner gifts it to him, he does not need another possession, because the possession of the gift is not dhaman, so the amanah possession substitutes for it because it is not dhaman. If he has a dhaman possession of the very item, which is dhaman by value or like, such as an item taken on approval to buy or a usurped item, then its owner gifts it to him, he does not need another possession, because it is in his hand by a dhaman possession, and the dhaman possession is higher than the possession that is not dhaman, so it substitutes for it. If he has an amanah and its owner sells it to him, he needs another possession, by going to the place where the item is and waiting a time in which he could take possession, because the possession of the sold item is dhaman, so the possession that is not dhaman does not substitute for it. And what is gifted to his minor child by the contract. [1] He said: 'And what is gifted...' etc.; meaning: the gift is completed by the contract, or the donor's gift to his child, because it is in the father's possession, so it substitutes for the minor's possession, because he is his guardian, so his possession is required. There is no difference in this between when the property is in his hand or in the hand of his depositee, because the hand of the depositee is like the hand of the owner. Contrary to when the father's property is mortgaged, usurped, or sold a fasid sale, the gift is not completed by the contract, because it is in the hand of another in the case of mortgage and usurpation, or in the ownership of another in the case of fasid sale, so the possession of the mortgagee and usurper does not substitute for the possession of the gift for the child. Al-Samni said in Kamal al-Dirayah: In al-Mabsut: He gifted to his son a known thing in his hand, it is permissible, and the father's possession is sufficient without the son's acceptance, and having witnesses is for choice and to guard against denial by the rest of the heirs after his death. And in al-Dhakhirah: Because every contract that one person undertakes is sufficient with the offer (ijab) like the father selling his property to his minor son. And if he gifts his child a house while he is living in it and his belongings are in it, the gift is valid and the son owns it by merely his saying 'I gift it to him', because it is in his hand, and his residence and belongings in it do not negate his hand over it, rather they confirm it, so the house is in his possession, which is the condition.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage covers two interrelated rulings. First, when a person gifts an item that is already in the donee's possession (e.g., a deposit, loan, or usurped item), no new physical possession (qabd) is required because the donee already holds the item. The commentator explains the principle: if the existing possession is homogeneous (e.g., both are amanah), one substitutes for the other. If the existing possession is of a higher type (dhaman) than the gift possession (non-dhaman), the higher substitutes for the lower. But if the existing possession is lower (amanah) and the new possession required is higher (dhaman, as in a sale), a new possession is needed. Second, when a father gifts something to his minor child, the father's own possession suffices for the child because he is the guardian. This applies even if the item is with the father's depositee. However, if the father's property is mortgaged, usurped, or sold fasidan (invalidly), the gift is not complete because the item is in the possession of another or in the ownership of another, so the father's possession does not suffice. The passage concludes with additional citations supporting that the father's possession suffices and that the father's residence in the gifted house does not prevent possession.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking possession; a condition for the completion of a gift in Hanafi fiqh.
أمانة (amanah): A trust possession where the holder is not liable for loss (e.g., deposit, loan for use).
مضمون (dhaman): A guaranteed possession where the holder is liable for loss (e.g., purchase, usurpation).
هبة (hibah): A gift; the transfer of ownership without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The gift is invalid because deposit possession cannot be used for gift.
B) The gift is valid without new possession because the existing amanah possession substitutes.
C) The gift requires new possession because the deposit is not the donee's property.
D) The gift is valid only if the donee returns the item first.

Key Takeaway
Existing possession (amanah or dhaman) can substitute for gift possession if homogeneous or higher; the father's hand is his minor child's hand.

Father's Gift to Minor Child: Conditions of Possession
Explains when possession is valid for a father's gift to minor child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of possession for a father's gift to his minor child and explain why the father's possession suffices.

Core Idea
A father's gift to his minor child is valid by contract alone if the father has possession; his hand is the child's hand.

In Hanafi fiqh, a gift (hiba) normally requires possession (qabd) by the recipient to be binding. However, when the donor is the father and the recipient is his minor child, a different rule applies. This lesson examines the conditions under which the father's possession fulfills the requirement, and when it does not. Understanding this exception is crucial for correctly executing gifts within the family.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: a father giving a gift (hiba) to his minor child. The ruling is that the gift is valid (sahih) upon the contract if the father has possession of the gifted item at the time of the gift.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for this ruling is the father's guardianship (wilaya) over the minor's property. The father has the authority to manage the child's affairs, and his possession substitutes for the child's possession. This is supported by the principle that the hand of the guardian is like the hand of the ward.
References from al-Mabsut and al-Dhakhira affirm this.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The connection between guardianship and the validity of the gift lies in the fact that the father is the legal representative of the child. Since the child cannot take possession, the father's possession fulfills the condition, analogous to his sale to the minor where acceptance is not required.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
This ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school (muttafaq alayh). No significant intra-Hanafi dispute is reported. Other schools like the Shafi'i may differ but are not discussed here.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the operative (mufta bihi) position. It is relied upon in fatwa without exception within the Hanafi school.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما وهب لطفله بالعقد
[1]-
وما وهب لطفله بالعقد
فلو كان في يد إنسان أمانة كالوديعة فوهبها مالكها فإنه لا يحتاج إلى قبض آخر ؛
لأنَّ قبض الهبة غير مضمون، فينوب عنه قبض الأمانة ؛ لكونه غير مضمون. ولو كان في يده مضمون بعينه، وهو المضمون بالقيمة أو المثل، كالمقبوض على
سوم البيع أو المغصوب، فوهبه مالكه منه، لا يحتاج إلى قبض آخر ؛ لأنه في يده بقبض
مضمون، والقبض المضمون أعلى من القبض الذي ليس بمضمون، فكان نائباً عنه. ولو كان في يده أمانة فباعها مالكها منه يحتاج إلى قبض آخر، بأن يرجع إلى موضع فيه العين، ويمضي وقت يتمكن فيه من قبضها ؛ لأن قبض المبيع مضمون، فلا
ينوب عنه القبض الذي ليس بمضمون. [١] قوله : ومـا وهـب... الخ ؛ أي وتم هبته بالعقد، أو هبة الواهب لطفله ؛ لأنه في قبض الأب، فينوب عن قبض الصغير؛ لأنه وليه ، فيشترط قبضه، ولا فرق في ذلك بين ما إذا كان في يده أو في يد مودعه ؛ لأن يد المودع كيد المالك.
بخلاف ما إذا كان مال الأب مرهوناً أو مغصوباً أو مبيعاً بيعاً فاسداً لم يتم الهبة بالعقد ؛ لأنه في يد غيره في صورة الرهن والغصب أو في ملك غيره في صورة البيع الفاسد، فلا ينوب قبض المرتهن والغاصب عن قبض الهبة للولد.
قال السمني في «كمال الدراية» (۱) : وفي (المبسوط»: وهب لابنه شيئاً معلوماً في يده جاز، وقبض الأب يكفي بلا قبوله، والإشهاد عليه للاختيار، والتحرز عن جحود سائر الورثة [بعد] موته.
وفي «الذخيرة»: لأنَّ كلَّ عقد يتولاه الواحد يكتفى فيه بالإيجاب كبيع الأب ماله لابنه الصغير، ولو وهب من طفله داراً وهو ساكنها ، ومتاعه فيها صحت الهبة وملكها الابن بمجرَّدِ قوله : وهبتُها له ؛ لأنها في يده، وسكناه ومتاعُه فيها لا ينافي يده عليها، بل
تقررها، فتكون الدار في قبضه وهو الشرط.
ولو كان ساكنها غيره بأجر لا تجوز الهبة ؛ لعدم قبض الأب ؛ لأنَّ قبض المستأجر لنفسه، ولو كان بغير أجرٍ جازت الهبة ، وملكها الابن بمجرد العقد. كذا عن «المنتقى».
انتهى
And what is given to his minor child by the contract... So if a person has an amanah (trust) like a deposit, and its owner gives it as a gift, then no new possession is needed; because the possession of a gift is not guaranteed (ghayr mamnun), so the trust possession suffices for it because it is not guaranteed. And if he has something that is guaranteed (mamnun) in itself, which is guaranteed by value or like, such as something taken on approval of sale or usurped property, and its owner gives it as a gift to him, no new possession is needed; because it is in his hand by a guaranteed possession, and the guaranteed possession is higher than the possession that is not guaranteed, so it suffices. And if he has a trust (amanah) and its owner sells it to him, a new possession is needed, i.e., he must return to the place where the item is and spend time enabling him to take possession; because the possession of a sold item is guaranteed, so the non-guaranteed possession does not suffice for it. [1] His statement: 'and what is given...' etc.; i.e., and his gift is completed by the contract, or the gift of the donor to his child; because it is in the father's possession, so he stands in for the minor's possession; because he is his guardian, so his possession is a condition. And there is no difference in this between whether it is in his hand or in the hand of his depositary; because the hand of the depositary is like the hand of the owner. Contrary to if the father's property is mortgaged, usurped, or sold with a corrupt sale, the gift is not completed by the contract; because it is in the hand of another in the case of mortgage and usurpation, or in the ownership of another in the case of corrupt sale, so the possession of the mortgagee and usurper does not substitute for the possession of the gift for the child. Al-Samani said in 'Kmal al-Diraya': And in al-Mabsut: He gave to his son a known thing in his hand, it is permissible, and the father's possession is sufficient without his acceptance, and having witnesses is optional, and cautions against denial by other heirs after his death. And in al-Dhakhira: Because every contract that is undertaken by one person is satisfied with the offer, like the father's sale of his property to his minor son. And if he gave his child a house and he is residing in it and his belongings are in it, the gift is valid and the son owns it by the mere utterance: 'I have given it to him'; because it is in his hand, and his residence and belongings in it do not negate his hand over it, but rather confirm it, so the house is in his possession, which is the condition. And if someone else resides in it with rent, the gift is not valid; due to lack of the father's possession; because the possession of the tenant is for himself. And if without rent, the gift is permissible, and the son owns it by the mere contract. Thus from al-Muntqa. End.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding the gift (hiba) of a father to his minor child. The main rule: if the father gives a gift to his minor child, the gift is completed by the contract without the need for the child's separate acceptance or possession, because the father's possession substitutes for the child's. The passage first discusses the nature of possession in gifts: trust possession (amanah) is not guaranteed (ghayr mamnun), while possession of a sale is guaranteed. For a gift, which is also non-guaranteed, existing trust possession suffices. If the property is already in the father's hand as a trust (e.g., deposit), no new possession is needed. If it is in his hand as a guaranteed possession (like a purchase on approval or usurped property), that also suffices because guaranteed possession is higher. However, if the property is in his hand as a trust and he buys it, he needs a new possession because sale possession is guaranteed. The commentary then applies this to the father: since the father is the guardian, his possession substitutes for the minor's. If the property is in the father's hand or in the hand of his depositary, it is valid. But if the property is mortgaged, usurped, or corruptly sold to another, the gift is not valid because the father does not have possession. The passage then cites al-Mabsut and al-Dhakhira affirming that the father's possession suffices and even if he is residing in the gifted house with his belongings, that constitutes possession. If the house is occupied by a tenant with rent, the father lacks possession and the gift fails; if without rent, the gift is valid.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): The taking of possession or control over a gifted item, which is a condition for the validity of the gift.
هبة (hiba): A contract of gift where ownership is transferred without compensation.
ولاية (wilaya): Guardianship authority over a minor, allowing the guardian to manage the minor's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a father gives a gift to his minor child, which of the following is true regarding possession (qabd)?
A) The father must take new possession if the property is already in his trust.
B) The father's existing trust possession suffices.
C) The child must take possession himself.
D) The gift is valid without any possession.

Key Takeaway
A father's gift to his minor child is valid upon contract if the father has possession; no separate acceptance or delivery is needed.

Accepting a Stranger's Gift for a Minor: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explains who can accept a stranger's gift for a minor and the Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a discerning minor's capacity to accept a gift and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on istihsan vs qiyas.

Core Idea
A discerning minor can accept a gift by istihsan, but Shafi'i rejects this by qiyas.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a stranger gives a gift to a minor who has no direct guardian present. Can the minor accept it himself, or must a guardian intervene? The Hanafi school, relying on istihsan, allows the discerning minor to accept, while Imam al-Shafi'i insists on guardian involvement based on qiyas. Understanding this dispute reveals how each school prioritizes different legal principles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the main ruling: the gift is valid if a discerning minor accepts it himself, or if a father, grandfather, executor, or custodian accepts on his behalf.
The minor must be 'aqil mumayyiz (discerning) for his own acceptance.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the minor's own acceptance is invalid. Cite his proof: qiyas on the general incapacity of minors.
The Shafi'i position is that guardianship cannot be bypassed before puberty.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response (istihsan)
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to distinguish pure benefit (manfa'a mahda) from ambiguous transactions, arguing that the minor's capacity is recognized for pure benefits.
Istihsan departs from the strict analogy (qiyas) that would otherwise block the minor's action.
4
Determine the practical consequence
In Hanafi law, the minor can accept gifts directly; in Shafi'i law, only a guardian can accept. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
The difference affects real-life transactions where the minor receives gifts from strangers.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author sides with the Hanafi position (mufta bihi) based on istihsan, citing supporting texts like al-Bahr and al-Mubtagha.
The Hanafi preference is clear: the discerning minor's own acceptance is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما وهب أجنبي له بقبضه عاقلاً ، أو قبض أبيه ، أو جده ، أو وصي أحدهما ،
أو أم هو معها (٢)
[1] قوله : وما وهب....الخ ؛ يعني وثم هبة ما وهبه الاجنبي لذلك الطفل بقبضه لو كان الطفل عاقلاً مميزاً يعقل التحصيل، ولو كان أبوه حياً ؛ لأنه في التصرف النافع يلحق بالبالغ استحساناً، وتتم أيضاً بقبض أبيه حال صغره أو جده أو وصي أحدهما سواء كان الطفل في حجرهم أو لا ؛ لأنَّ لهؤلاء ولاية عليه ؛ أما الأب فظاهر، وأمّا غيره من الجد والوصي فلقيامهم مقام الأب.
وقال الشافعي له : لا يصح قبض الصغير بنفسه وإن كان عاقلاً، وهو القياس؛ لأنه لا اعتبار بعقله قبل البلوغ ؛ لأنَّ الولاية عليه لا تزول عنه قبله ، فلو اعتبر بعقله وجعل له ولاية التصرّف لما نفذ تصرُّف غيره، ولما كان له حاجة إليه.
ووجه الاستحسان : إن عدم اعتبارِ عقله قبل البلوغ للنظر له ودفع الضرر عنه ، وذلك فيما كان متردّداً بين النفع والضرر، وأما النفعُ المحض فيعتبر عقله فيه، ويلحقُ
بالبالغ كما في كسبه للمباحات ؛ ولذا قال في «البحر»
لصغير يعبر عن نفسه شيئاً فرده يصح، كما يصح قبوله. انتهى
عن «المبتغى»: من وهب
[٢] قوله : أو أم هو معها...الخ ؛ أي وتتم هبة أجنبي للطفل بقبض أم أو أجنبي يربيه سواء كان قريباً أو أجنبيّاً لو كان ذلك الطفل في حجرهما ؛ لأنَّ من هو في يده ولاية التصرف النافع له ، وتحصيل المال من أوفر المنافع. فكان ذلك لثبوتِ يدهم عليه ، حتى لا يكون لغيرهم أن ينزعه من أيديهم،
And what a stranger gave to him by his possession if he is rational, or by the possession of his father, or his grandfather, or the executor of either, or a mother who is with him. [1] His statement: 'and what is given...' etc.; i.e., and then the gift of what the stranger gave to that child is completed by his possession if the child is rational and discerning, capable of understanding acquisition, even if his father is alive; because in pure beneficial transactions he is treated like an adult by istihsan. And it is also completed by the possession of his father during his minority, or his grandfather, or the executor of either, whether the child is under their care or not; because these have guardianship over him. As for the father, it is obvious, and as for others like grandfather and executor, they stand in the father's place. And al-Shafi'i said to him: The minor's own possession is not valid even if he is rational, and this is the qiyas; because his intellect is not considered before puberty; because guardianship over him does not cease before that. So if his intellect were considered and he were given authority to transact, then the authority of others would not be effective, and he would have no need for them. And the face of istihsan: The non-consideration of his intellect before puberty is for his benefit and to repel harm from him, and that is in matters that are ambiguous between benefit and harm. As for pure benefit, his intellect is considered in it, and he is treated like an adult, just as in his acquisition of permissible things. Hence it is said in 'al-Bahr': A minor who expresses something himself, his rejection is valid, just as his acceptance is valid. End. From 'al-Mubtagha': Whoever gives... [2] His statement: 'or a mother who is with him...' etc.; i.e., and the gift of a stranger to the child is completed by the possession of a mother or a stranger who raises him, whether the child is a relative or not, if the child is in their care; because the one in whose hand he is has the authority of beneficial disposition for him, and acquiring wealth is among the most beneficial. So this is due to their established hand over him, so that no one else can take him from their hands, ...
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an inter-madhhab dispute regarding a gift from a non-relative (stranger) to a minor child. The Hanafi position: The gift is valid if the minor is discerning (mumayyiz) and accepts it himself—this is by istihsan because it is a pure benefit for the minor. Alternatively, the gift can be accepted by the child's father, grandfather, or the executor of either, regardless of whether the child is in their custody. The mother or a stranger who raises the child can also accept if the child is in their care, because they have authority over beneficial transactions. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a minor cannot accept a gift on his own, even if discerning, based on qiyas: the minor's capacity is not recognized before puberty, and guardianship remains. The Hanafi response (istihsan) distinguishes matters of pure benefit from those involving potential loss; for pure benefit, the minor's own action is valid, just as they can acquire mubahat. Thus the Hanafi position is preferred (mufta bihi) based on istihsan.

Key Terms
مميز (mumayyiz): A minor who has reached an age of discretion and can distinguish between benefit and harm.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that favors a ruling due to its suitability or equity over strict analogy.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a common effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a discerning minor (mumayyiz) accept a gift from a stranger by himself?
A) Yes, because it is pure benefit.
B) No, only the guardian can accept.
C) Yes, but only if the father is dead.
D) No, because the minor has no capacity.

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor can accept a gift of pure benefit by himself under Hanafi law, based on istihsan, while the Shafi'i school requires guardian acceptance based on qiyas.

Acceptance of a Gift for a Minor: The Hanafi Istiḥsān vs. Shāfi‘ī Qiyās
Ruling on who may accept a gift on behalf of a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who may accept a gift on behalf of a minor according to the Hanafi school and explain why the Hanafi position permits a discerning minor to accept directly based on istiḥsān.

Core Idea
A discerning minor can accept a pure-benefit gift himself by istiḥsān, unlike the Shāfi‘ī strict qiyās.

Giving a gift to a minor raises the question: who can accept it? The Hanafi school, considering the minor's welfare, allows the minor himself if discerning, and also various guardians. This lesson unpacks the passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah which lists the authorized persons and then presents the dispute with Imām al-Shāfi‘ī over the minor's own acceptance. We will see how istiḥsān (juristic preference) overrides strict analogy in matters of pure benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and list the authorized persons
Read the passage to extract the list: the minor himself if intelligent and discerning (ʿāqil mumayyiz), his father, grandfather, executor, mother (if the child is with her), a stranger custodian, and the husband of a minor girl after the wedding. Understand that each can take possession (qabḍ) to complete the gift.
The Hanafi school considers the minor's own acceptance valid based on istiḥsān, not qiyās.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof (istiḥsān) for the minor's own acceptance
The reasoning: a discerning minor is like an adult in purely beneficial matters (nafʿ maḥḍ), similar to his ability to acquire permissible things (kash al-mubāḥāt). The rationale for not considering his intelligence in ambiguous matters is to protect him from harm; but in pure benefit, there is no harm, so his intelligence suffices.
This is a departure from strict qiyās which would require a guardian.
3
Examine the Shāfi‘ī counter-argument (qiyās)
Imām al-Shāfi‘ī argues that a minor's intelligence is not considered before puberty because guardianship remains. Allowing him to transact would make guardianship unnecessary. This is the qiyās (analogy) that the Hanafi overcome with istiḥsān.
The Shāfi‘ī position requires a guardian (father, grandfather, etc.) to accept the gift.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
In practice, the Hanafi ruling allows a discerning minor to accept a gift directly, while the Shāfi‘ī ruling requires an adult guardian to accept. This affects real transactions where no guardian is present or the child receives a gift personally.
The Hanafi view is muftā bihi (relied upon for fatwa).
5
Apply to scenarios of other guardians
Note that the mother or a stranger custodian can accept if the child is in their care, and the husband of a minor girl can accept after the wedding. These are also considered instances of pure benefit where the custodian or husband acts for the child's welfare.
The father's presence does not prevent the husband's acceptance after the wedding, as the father delegated authority.

وما وهب أجنبي له بقبضه عاقلاً ، أو قبض أبيه ، أو جده ، أو وصي أحدهما ، أو أم هو معها
ولو كان ساكنها غيره بأجر لا تجوز الهبة ؛ لعدم قبض الأب ؛ لأنَّ قبض المستأجر لنفسه، ولو كان بغير أجرٍ جازت الهبة ، وملكها الابن بمجرد العقد. كذا عن «المنتقى».
انتهى
[1] قوله : وما وهب....الخ ؛ يعني وثم هبة ما وهبه الاجنبي لذلك الطفل بقبضه لو كان الطفل عاقلاً مميزاً يعقل التحصيل، ولو كان أبوه حياً ؛ لأنه في التصرف النافع يلحق بالبالغ استحساناً، وتتم أيضاً بقبض أبيه حال صغره أو جده أو وصي أحدهما سواء كان الطفل في حجرهم أو لا ؛ لأنَّ لهؤلاء ولاية عليه ؛ أما الأب فظاهر، وأمّا غيره من الجد والوصي فلقيامهم مقام الأب.
وقال الشافعي له : لا يصح قبض الصغير بنفسه وإن كان عاقلاً، وهو القياس؛ لأنه لا اعتبار بعقله قبل البلوغ ؛ لأنَّ الولاية عليه لا تزول عنه قبله ، فلو اعتبر بعقله وجعل له ولاية التصرّف لما نفذ تصرُّف غيره، ولما كان له حاجة إليه.
ووجه الاستحسان : إن عدم اعتبارِ عقله قبل البلوغ للنظر له ودفع الضرر عنه ، وذلك فيما كان متردّداً بين النفع والضرر، وأما النفعُ المحض فيعتبر عقله فيه، ويلحق بالبالغ كما في كسبه للمباحات ؛ ولذا قال في «البحر»: لصغير يعبر عن نفسه شيئاً فرده يصح، كما يصح قبوله. انتهى
[٢] قوله : أو أم هو معها...الخ ؛ أي وتتم هبة أجنبي للطفل بقبض أم أو أجنبي يربيه سواء كان قريباً أو أجنبيّاً لو كان ذلك الطفل في حجرهما ؛ لأنَّ من هو في يده ولاية التصرف النافع له ، وتحصيل المال من أوفر المنافع. فكان ذلك لثبوتِ يدهم عليه ، حتى لا يكون لغيرهم أن ينزعه من أيديهم،
أو أجنبي يُربيه وهو معه، أو زوجها لها بعد الزفاف أو أجنبي يُربيه وهو معه ، أو زوجها لها بعد الزفاف) : أي زوج الطفل " الموهوب لها لأجلها لكن بعد الزفاف.
فكانوا أحق بحفظه وتحصيل المال له من ضروراتِ حفظه ؛ لأنه لا يبقى عادة إلا بفوت ملبوس، فقاموا في هذا مقام الولي عند عدم الولي ؛ لكونه نفعاً محضاً). [11] قوله : أو زوجها لها ؛ يعني أو تتم هبة ما وهبه للطفلة بقبض زوجها، ولو مع حضرة الأب بعدما زُفّت الطفلة إليه في الصحيح ؛ لأنَّ الأب أقامه مقام نفسه في حفظها، وقبض الهبة منه.
ولو قبضه الأب أيضاً صح؛ لأنَّ الولاية له، واشتراط الزفاف لثبوت ولاية الزوج ؛ لأنها إنما يملكه باعتبار أنه يعولها ، وذلك بعد الزفاف، فلا يصح قبض الزوج قبل الزفاف؛ لأنه الا يعولها قبله ولا يشترط أن يكون مما يجامع مثلها في الصحيح، صرح به الزيلعي وغيره، ومن ثمّ أطلقه المصنف له في «المتن»، ولو قبضت نفسها وهي تعقل جاز. كما صرحوا به.
(٢) قوله : زوج الطفل ؛ الطفلُ الولد الصغير من الإنسان والدواب، قال ابنُ الأَنْبَارِي : ويكون الطفلُ بلفظ واحد للمذكر والمؤنث والجمع، قال الله : أو الطفلِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يَظْهَرُوا عَلَى عَوْرَاتِ النِّسَاءِ.
و يجوز المطابقة في التثنية والجمع والتأنيث فيقال: طفلة وطفلات وأطفال، وكل أنثى إذا ولدت فهي طفل ، قال بعضهم : ويبقى هذا الاسم للولد حتى يميّز، ثمّ لا يقال : بعد ذلك طفل بل صبي وحَزَوّر ويافع ومُراهق وبالغ، وفي «التهذيب»: يقال له طفل إلى أن يحتلم. كذا في «المصباح المنير».
The Book of Gift / Its Definition and Rulings
'And what a stranger gives to him [the minor] by his own possession, being intelligent, or by the possession of his father, or his grandfather, or the executor of either of them, or a mother he is with.'
'And if the dweller of it is someone else with rent, the gift is not valid due to the lack of the father's possession, because the possession of the lessee is for himself; but if without rent, the gift is valid, and the son owns it merely by the contract. Thus from al-Muntaqa.' End.
[1] His statement: 'And what a stranger gives...' etc.; meaning: and also the gift of what a stranger gives to that child by his possession if the child is intelligent, discerning, understanding acquisition, even if his father is alive, because in beneficial transactions he is considered as an adult by istihsan. It is also completed by the possession of his father during his minority, or his grandfather, or the executor of either of them, whether the child is in their custody or not, because they have guardianship over him. As for the father, it is evident; as for others like the grandfather and executor, they stand in the place of the father.
Al-Shafi'i said: The possession of a minor by himself is not valid even if he is intelligent, and that is qiyas, because his intelligence is not considered before puberty, since guardianship over him does not cease before it. If his intelligence were considered and he were given authority to transact, the transaction of others would not be effective and there would be no need for a guardian over him.
The face of istihsan: Not considering his intelligence before puberty is for his welfare and to repel harm from him, and that is in what is between benefit and harm. As for pure benefit, his intelligence is considered, and he is treated like an adult, as in his acquisition of permissible things. Hence, it is stated in al-Bahr: 'If a minor expresses something himself, returning it is valid, just as accepting it is valid.' End.
[2] His statement: 'or a mother he is with...' etc.; meaning: The gift of a stranger to a child is completed by the possession of a mother or a stranger who raises him, whether he is a relative or a stranger, if that child is in their custody, because whoever is in their hand has authority over beneficial transactions for him, and acquisition of wealth is among the most important benefits. Thus it is due to their hand being over him, such that no one else may remove him from their hands.
'Or a stranger who raises him while he is with him, or her husband after wedding, or a stranger who raises him while he is with him, or her husband after wedding): i.e., the husband of the child-girl, the gifted one for her sake, but after wedding.
They are more entitled to his preservation and acquisition of wealth due to the necessities of his preservation, because he usually does not remain except by the loss of clothing [i.e., needs maintenance]. So they stand in the place of the guardian when there is no guardian, because it is pure benefit.
[11] His statement: 'or her husband after wedding'; meaning: The gift given to a girl is completed by the possession of her husband, even with the presence of the father, after she has been sent (zuffat) to him in the correct view, because the father has appointed him in his place for her preservation and for taking possession of the gift.
If the father also takes possession, it is valid, because guardianship is his, and the condition of wedding is for the establishment of the husband's guardianship, because he only owns her maintenance after wedding. Thus, the husband's possession before wedding is not valid, because he does not maintain her before it. It is not required that he be capable of intercourse with someone like her in the correct view, as stated by al-Zayla'i and others. Hence, the author in al-Matn left it unrestricted. And if she takes possession herself while she is intelligent, it is valid, as they stated.
(2) His statement: 'husband of the child'; al-tifl (child) is a small offspring of humans and animals. Ibn al-Anbari said: The word tifl can be used in one form for masculine, feminine, and plural. Allah said: 'or children who have not yet seen the private parts of women' (al-Nur: 31). It is permissible to match in dual, plural, and feminine, so one says: tifla (girl), tiflat (girls), atfal (children). Every female when born is a tifl. Some said: This name remains for the child until he becomes discerning; then after that, one does not say 'tifl' but rather sabi, hazawar, yafi', murahiq, baligh. In al-Tahdhib: It is called tifl until he reaches puberty. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the ruling regarding acceptance of a gift (hiba) for a minor (tifl). The passage type is a ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute. The author first quotes the original matn which lists the persons whose possession (qabd) completes the gift for a minor: the minor himself if intelligent (aqil), his father, grandfather, executor of either, or the mother with whom he lives. The commentator then expands: the minor must be intelligent and discerning (mumayyiz) so that he understands acquisition. If so, based on istihsan (juristic preference), he is treated like an adult in purely beneficial transactions, even if his father is alive. The gift is also complete by the father's possession, the grandfather's, or the executor's, as they have guardianship (wilaya). The commentator then presents a dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the minor's own possession is invalid because his intelligence is not considered before puberty, and guardianship remains. This is the qiyas (analogy). The Hanafi response explains that istihsan is based on the fact that not considering his intelligence is for his welfare in ambiguous matters, but for pure benefit (naf' mahd), his intelligence is considered as in his acquisition of permissible things. This is supported by a statement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. Next, the commentator addresses the mother or a stranger (ajnabi) who raises the child. If the child is in their custody (in their hand), they can accept the gift because they have effective authority over beneficial transactions for the child. They stand in the place of the guardian when there is none, as it is pure benefit. Then the case of a girl married to a husband: after she is sent to him (after wedding, zafaf), the husband can accept the gift on her behalf, even if the father is present, because the father has appointed him. However, before the wedding, the husband cannot accept because he does not yet maintain her. The father can also accept. The girl herself can accept if she is intelligent. Finally, a linguistic note clarifies the definition of 'tifl' (child) from various sources, stating it applies until puberty.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): A gift: transfer of ownership without compensation.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or legal possession; completion of a gift requires qabd by the donee or his representative.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: departing from analogical reasoning (qiyas) for a more equitable ruling based on necessity or custom.
طفل (tifl): A young child, from birth until puberty or until discernment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid person to accept a gift for a minor according to the Hanafi school?
A) The minor himself if discerning
B) The father
C) A neighbour who does not have the child in his care
D) The husband of a minor girl after the wedding

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a discerning minor to accept a gift directly by istiḥsān, and allows various guardians (father, grandfather, mother, custodian, husband) to accept when the child is in their care, all based on the principle of pure benefit.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Gift by One Person to Two Donees
Intra-Hanafi dispute on gift to two donees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa invalidates a single gift to two donees and evaluate the Sahibayn's counter-argument using qiyas with rahn.

Core Idea
A single gift to two donees is invalid per Abu Hanifa due to shuyu; the Sahibayn allow it by analogy to pawn.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a classical intra-Hanafi disagreement: can one person give a single house to two donees as a joint gift? Abu Hanifa holds it invalid because it creates an undivided share (shuyu), while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it valid, drawing an analogy to a pawn given to two creditors. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify the key issue of shuyu in contracts and to evaluate analogical reasoning (qiyas) within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the problem and the two positions
Identify the scenario: one donor, two donees, one house. Note that Abu Hanifa says the gift is invalid (fasid), while the Sahibayn say it is valid (sahih).
The opposite case – two donors giving to one donee – is unanimously valid.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Their argument: the transfer of ownership (tamlik) is a single act, so no shuyu arises. They analogize to a valid pawn (rahn) of one item to two creditors – even though the pawn creates co-ownership of the security, it is valid. They claim the gift is even more worthy of validity because shuyu is less problematic in gifts than in pawns.
The analogy: asl = pawn of one item to two creditors (valid); far' = gift of one house to two donees; illa = single possession without division.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof and response
Abu Hanifa counters that the gift is effectively two separate gifts – half from each donee – because ownership is established in an undivided half for each. This creates shuyu at the time of possession, which invalidates the gift. He rejects the analogy to pawn because in pawn each creditor has a complete right of detention over the entire property, so no shuyu exists in the rights; in gift, ownership is truly shared.
Key evidence: if the gifted item cannot be divided and one donee accepts, the gift is valid – implying it is two gifts of halves.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the gift must be re-done individually (two separate gifts). Under the Sahibayn, one single transaction suffices.
This difference affects simplicity of transactions and potential disputes over division.
5
Determine the tarjih (preferred view)
The text does not explicitly state the mufta bihi, but later Hanafi sources often prefer Abu Hanifa's view. However, the commentary here presents both arguments neutrally. As a student, you should be able to state both positions and the reasoning behind each.
In many later works, the fatwa is given according to Abu Hanifa due to caution regarding shuyu.

Source Text

كتاب الهبة / تعريفها وأحكامها
هبة اثنين دار الواحد، وعكسه لا
وصح هبة اثنين دارَ الواحد) ؛ لأنَّ الكل يقع في يده بلا شيوع، (وعكسُهُ لا ) : أي هبة واحدٍ لاثنين داراً لا تصح عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما تصح ؛ لأنَّ التمليك واحد فلا شيوع، كما إذا رَهَنَ من رجلين. وله : أن هذه هبةُ النّصف من كلِّ واحد، فيثبت الشيوع)، بخلاف
[٤]
(۲)
الرهن ؛ لأنه محبوس بدين كل واحد بكماله.
[ ١ ] قوله : لأن الكل ... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ الواهبينَ سلّما تلك الدار جملة، والموهوب له قبضها جملة، ولا شيوع في ذلك، فإنّ المؤثر هو الشيوع عند القبض له عند العقد، ألا ترى أنه لـو وهـب الكل ثمَّ سلّم النصف لا يجوز، ولو وهب النصف ثم النصف
وسلم الشكل يجوز. [٢] قوله : لأن التمليك واحد ... الخ ؛ هذا دليل على مذهبهما، وتقريره : إنّ هذه هبة الجملة من الواهبين ؛ لكون التمليك واحداً ، فلا يتحقق الشيوع كما إذا رهن من رجلين، فإنّه يجوز، بل الهبة أولى بالجواز ؛ لأنَّ تأثير الشيوع في الرهن أقوى منه في الهبة، حتى امتنع رهن المشاع فيما لا يحتمل القسمة دون الهبة. (۳) قوله : وله ... الخ ؛ هذا دليل على مذهب الإمام، وتقريره : إن هذه هبة النصف
من كل واحد منهما. ألا ترى أنَّ هبة الواحد للاثنين لو كانت فيما لا يقسم فقبل أحدهما صح، فصار كما لو وهب النصف لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما بعقد على حدة، على أن الملك يثبت لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما في النصف، وهو غير ممتاز، فكان الشيوع هو يمنع القبض على سبيل الكمال ، فتحقق الشيوعُ لثبوته في التمليك بثبوت الملك مشاعاً، فالهبة تفسد. [٤] قوله : بخلاف الرهن.... الخ ؛ هذا جواب من الإمام عما استشهدا به الحكم، تقريره : إن قياس الهبة على الرهن في هذا الحكم قياس مع الفارق ؛ لأنَّ حكم الرهنِ
هو حبس المرهون، وهو ثابت لكلّ واحدٍ من للمرتهنين كاملاً ، فلا يتحقق الشيوع.
(۱) وبيانه : إن تمليك الكل منهما تمليك البعض الشائع من كل منهما ؛ لأنه لا وجه له سوى هذا، وهذا باطل. ينظر : رمز الحقائق (۲ : ١٨٦).
The Book of Gift / Its Definition and Rulings. Gift of two persons of a house to one, and its opposite is not valid. And the gift of two persons of a house to one is valid because the whole falls into his hand without undivided share (shuyu). And its opposite is not valid: i.e., the gift of one person to two persons of a house is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is valid because the transfer of ownership is one, so there is no shuyu, just as if he pawned (rahana) from two men. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that this is a gift of half from each one, so shuyu is established, contrary to pawning because it is held as security for the debt of each one in full. [1] His saying: because the whole... etc.; meaning that the two donors delivered that house as a whole, and the donee took possession of it as a whole, and there is no shuyu in that, for the effective factor is shuyu at the time of possession, not at the contract. Do you not see that if he gifted the whole then delivered half, it is not permissible, and if he gifted half then half and delivered the whole, it is permissible. [2] His saying: because the transfer of ownership is one... etc.; this is the proof of their (Sahibayn's) school, and its explanation: this is a gift of the whole from the two donors, because the transfer of ownership is one, so shuyu does not materialize, just as if he pawned from two men, it is permissible. Rather, the gift is more worthy of permissibility because the effect of shuyu in pawning is stronger than in gift, to the extent that pawning of undivided property that does not admit division is prohibited, while gift of it is not. (3) His saying: and for him... etc.; this is the proof of the Imam's (Abu Hanifa's) school, and its explanation: this is a gift of half from each one. Do you not see that the gift of one person to two persons, if it were in something that cannot be divided, and one of them accepted, it would be valid, so it becomes as if he gifted half to each one by separate contracts, such that ownership is established for each one in half, which is not distinguished. Therefore, shuyu prevents possession in a complete manner, so shuyu is realized due to its establishment in the transfer of ownership by establishing ownership as undivided, so the gift is void. [4] His saying: contrary to pawning... etc.; this is a response from the Imam (Abu Hanifa) to the ruling they cited as evidence. Its explanation: that analogizing the gift to pawning in this ruling is an analogy with a difference, because the ruling of pawning is the detention of the pawned item, and it is established for each of the pawnbrokers completely, so shuyu does not materialize. (1) Its explanation: that transferring ownership of the whole from both of them is a transfer of a part of the undivided share from each of them, because there is no way other than this, and this is invalid. See: Ramz al-Haqa'iq (2:186).
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute from the commentary on the gift of one person to two donees. It presents three positions: (1) It is valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) because the transfer of ownership (tamlik) is single, so no undivided share (shuyu) arises. They analogize to a pawn (rahn) given to two creditors, which is valid. (2) It is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because it is effectively a gift of half from each donee, leading to shuyu. He argues that the gift is actually two separate transfers, each of an undivided half. (3) The text then refutes the analogy to pawn: in pawn, the right of detention (habs) is complete for each creditor, so no shuyu; in gift, ownership is shared, causing shuyu. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but the commentary explains both positions. The passage also clarifies that the effective factor is shuyu at the time of possession (qabd), not at the contract.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): A gift; immediate transfer of ownership without compensation.
شيوع (shuyu): Undivided share or co-ownership; the state of an item being owned jointly without physical division.
رهن (rahn): Pawn; a contract where property is held as security for a debt.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; the act of making someone owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does Abu Hanifa consider a gift by one person to two donees invalid?
A) Because the gift is not divided at the contract
B) Because it results in an undivided share (shuyu)
C) Because two donees cannot jointly own a house
D) Because the delivery must be separate

Key Takeaway
The core of the dispute is whether a single transfer of ownership (tamlik) creates an undivided share (shuyu) that invalidates the gift; Abu Hanifa says yes, the Sahibayn say no.
