Gift to Multiple Donees: The Shuyu' Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hiba to multiple donees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hiba from one donor to two donees and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement using the concept of shuyu'.

Core Idea
Co-ownership at possession invalidates gift; but is one transfer to two a single act or two halves?

In Hanafi fiqh, a gift (hiba) requires valid taking of possession (qabd). Co-ownership (shuyu') at the time of possession can invalidate the gift because each co-owner cannot exclusively possess the whole. This passage examines a specific scenario: a single donor giving a house to two donees. The ruling splits the school—Abu Hanifa invalidates it, while his two companions uphold it. Let's explore the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the undisputed ruling
The passage begins by stating that a gift from two donors to one donee is valid. Confirm why: the donee receives the entire property without co-ownership.
This is agreed upon by all.
2
State the disputed case
The opposite case: a gift from one donor to two donees. Note that Abu Hanifa says it is invalid, while the two companions say it is valid.
3
Analyze the companions' proof
The companions argue that the transfer is a single act of ownership, so no co-ownership arises. They analogize to pawning (rahn) to two creditors, which is valid. They claim pawning has a stronger shuyu' effect, yet it is allowed, so gift should be even more permissible.
Their dalil is qiyas with rahn.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the gift is effectively two separate transfers: half to each donee. This creates undivided co-ownership (shuyu') at possession, which invalidates the gift because each donee cannot take complete possession. He distinguishes pawning: in rahn, the entire property secures each creditor's full debt, so no partial ownership exists.
His rebuttal is a qiyas ma'a al-fariq.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the gift is void; under the companions, it is valid if possession is taken. The matn of al-Wiqayah follows Abu Hanifa, but later practice may adopt the companions' view.
Fatwa hierarchy not explicitly given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هبة اثنين دار الواحد، وعكسه لا
وصح هبة اثنين دارَ الواحد)؛ لأنَّ الكل يقع في يده بلا شيوع، (وعكسُهُ لا): أي هبة واحدٍ لاثنين داراً لا تصح عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما تصح؛ لأنَّ التمليك واحد فلا شيوع، كما إذا رَهَنَ من رجلين. وله: أن هذه هبةُ النّصف من كلِّ واحد، فيثبت الشيوع)، بخلاف الرهن؛ لأنه محبوس بدين كل واحد بكماله.
[ ١ ] قوله: لأن الكل ... الخ؛ يعني إنَّ الواهبينَ سلّما تلك الدار جملة، والموهوب له قبضها جملة، ولا شيوع في ذلك، فإنّ المؤثر هو الشيوع عند القبض له عند العقد، ألا ترى أنه لـو وهـب الكل ثمَّ سلّم النصف لا يجوز، ولو وهب النصف ثم النصف وسلم الشكل يجوز. [٢] قوله: لأن التمليك واحد ... الخ؛ هذا دليل على مذهبهما، وتقريره: إنّ هذه هبة الجملة من الواهبين؛ لكون التمليك واحداً، فلا يتحقق الشيوع كما إذا رهن من رجلين، فإنّه يجوز، بل الهبة أولى بالجواز؛ لأنَّ تأثير الشيوع في الرهن أقوى منه في الهبة، حتى امتنع رهن المشاع فيما لا يحتمل القسمة دون الهبة. (۳) قوله: وله ... الخ؛ هذا دليل على مذهب الإمام، وتقريره: إن هذه هبة النصف من كل واحد منهما. ألا ترى أنَّ هبة الواحد للاثنين لو كانت فيما لا يقسم فقبل أحدهما صح، فصار كما لو وهب النصف لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما بعقد على حدة، على أن الملك يثبت لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما في النصف، وهو غير ممتاز، فكان الشيوع هو يمنع القبض على سبيل الكمال، فتحقق الشيوعُ لثبوته في التمليك بثبوت الملك مشاعاً، فالهبة تفسد. [٤] قوله: بخلاف الرهن.... الخ؛ هذا جواب من الإمام عما استشهدا به الحكم، تقريره: إن قياس الهبة على الرهن في هذا الحكم قياس مع الفارق؛ لأنَّ حكم الرهنِ هو حبس المرهون، وهو ثابت لكلّ واحدٍ من المرتهنين كاملاً، فلا يتحقق الشيوع.
A gift of two (people) of a house to one (person) is valid, and the opposite is not. The gift of two to one is valid because the whole property comes into his hand without co-ownership. The opposite, i.e., a gift of one to two of a house, is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two companions it is valid because the transfer of ownership is one, so there is no co-ownership, like when one pawns (something) to two men. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is the proof that this is a gift of half from each, so co-ownership is established, unlike pawning because it is held as security for the full debt of each one. [1] His saying: 'because the whole...' etc.; meaning that the two donors handed over that entire house collectively, and the donee received it collectively, and there is no co-ownership in that; indeed the effective factor is co-ownership at the time of taking possession, not at the time of the contract. Do you not see that if he gave the whole then handed over half, it is not valid; and if he gave half then half and handed over the whole, it is valid. [2] His saying: 'because the transfer is one...' etc.; this is a proof for their (the two companions') school, and its explanation: this is a gift of the whole from the two donors because the transfer is one, so co-ownership does not occur, as when one pawns from two men, for it is valid. Rather, the gift is more deserving of validity because the effect of co-ownership in pawning is stronger than in gift, even to the extent that pawning of a share (musha') in something not subject to division is prohibited unlike gift. (3) His saying: 'and for him...' etc.; this is a proof for the Imam's school, and its explanation: this is a gift of half from each of them. Do you not see that if a gift from one to two were in something undivided and one of them accepted, it would be valid, so it becomes as if he gave half to each of them in a separate contract; so ownership is established for each of them in half, and it is indistinguishable, and co-ownership prevents taking possession completely, so co-ownership is established due to its establishment in the transfer by the establishment of ownership in common, so the gift is invalid. [4] His saying: 'unlike pawning...' etc.; this is a response from the Imam to what they cited as evidence for the ruling, its explanation: the analogy of gift to pawning in this ruling is an analogy with a difference; because the ruling of pawning is the retention of the pawned item, and it is established for each of the mortgagees in full, so co-ownership does not occur.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute on gifts involving multiple parties. The basic ruling is that a gift from two donors to a single donee is valid because the donee receives the entire property without any co-ownership (shuyu'). Conversely, a gift from one donor to two donees is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but valid according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The rationale: According to Abu Hanifa, each donee receives half of the property, resulting in co-ownership (shuyu'), which prevents valid taking of possession (qabd) as required for a gift. The two companions argue that the transfer is one and the same act, so no co-ownership arises, analogizing it to a pawn (rahn) given to two creditors, which is valid. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes: in pawning, the property is held as security for the full debt of each, so no partial ownership exists; whereas in a gift, each donee owns his share, creating co-ownership. The commentary (sharh) elaborates on each point, confirming that the disagreement revolves around the meaning of co-ownership and its effect on possession.

Key Terms
شيوع (shuyu'): Co-ownership or undivided share in a property; the state of being shared such that each owner's portion is not physically demarcated.
هبة (hiba): A gift; a contract of transfer of ownership without compensation, requiring offer, acceptance, and possession.
رهن (rahn): A pawn or pledge; a contract where property is given as security for a debt, retained by the creditor during the term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason Imam Abu Hanifa gives for invalidating a gift from one donor to two donees?
A) The gift must be written and witnessed.
B) The transfer creates co-ownership (shuyu') which prevents valid possession.
C) The donees must be relatives of the donor.
D) The property must be divisible.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a gift to multiple donees hinges on whether the transfer creates co-ownership at possession, a point of legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Giving Charity to Multiple Recipients: The Hanafi Dispute on Co-ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sadaqa to multiple recipients with qiyas to hiba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on sadaqa to two rich vs two poor and apply the concept of niyaba to determine validity.

Core Idea
Charity to the poor is valid for multiple recipients because they receive as deputies of Allah, avoiding co-ownership.

In this passage, the author (al-Marghinani in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya) examines a classic Hanafi ikhtilaf: is giving a single sum of charity to two rich people valid? The ruling hinges on whether the transaction is treated as pure charity (sadaqa) or as a gift (hiba) due to the recipients' wealth. The key distinction lies in the concept of niyaba (deputyship): the poor act as deputies for Allah, so no co-ownership arises, whereas the rich receive as themselves, causing shuyu'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and recipients
The act is giving ten dirhams as sadaqa to two people. Two cases: rich (aghniya') and poor (fuqara').
The ruling differs based on the recipients' wealth.
2
State the ruling per Abu Hanifa's two narrations
Original narration (riwayat al-asl): both sadaqa and hiba to two rich are invalid. Narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir: sadaqa to two poor is valid; sadaqa to two rich is invalid.
These two narrations show an internal difference within Abu Hanifa's own positions.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider sadaqa to two rich valid because they treat it as hiba, which is valid in their view due to no co-ownership.
Sahibayn do not recognize shuyu' as an invalidating factor in hiba.
4
Analyze the proof: hadith and niyaba
The hadith states that charity falls into Allah's hand first. This shows the poor are deputies; no shuyu' because the recipient is Allah, not multiple humans. Compare to hiba where ownership transfers directly to the donees.
Niyaba is the key concept that resolves the apparent contradiction.
5
Apply the framework to a new scenario
If you give a gift (hiba) to two rich people, it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa due to shuyu'. According to Sahibayn, it is valid because they do not see shuyu' as a problem.
This step tests understanding of the underlying principles.

[١] قوله: كتصدق عشرة...الخ؛ وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له في رواية «الجامع الصغير»، وعندهما يصح على غنيين أيضاً، ففرق أبو حنيفة له على هذه الرواية بين الصدقة: وهو ما يدفع إلى الفقير ولو كان بلفظ الهبة وبين الهبة وهو ما يدفع إلى الغنى ولو كان بلفظ الصدقة. وسوى بينهما في عدم الصحة في رواية الأصل حيث قال عقـب مسألة الهبة: وكذلك الصدقة: أي لا تجوز عند أبي حنيفة الله على رجلين، كما لا تجوز الهبة، والأظهر أن عنه في المسألة روايتين: فوجه رواية الأصل أن تمام الصدقة بالقبض، وكل واحد يلاقي شائعاً فلا يتم به الصدقة كما لا يتم به الهبة. ووجه رواية «الجامع»: إنّ الصدقة يراد بها وجه الله تعالى؛ ولهذا لم يكن فيها رجوع، وإنما تقع للفقيرين بطريق النيابة، والله واحد لا شريك له، فلا يتمكن الشيوع فيها، وصارت كالهبة إذا وقعت لواحد وقبضها اثنان بحكم النيابة، والهبة يراد به الغنيان فيقع فيها الشيوع.
Like giving charity of ten (dirhams) to two rich people, and it is valid for two poor people: i.e., if one gives charity of ten to two rich people, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise if he gives a gift to them due to co-ownership. According to the two companions, the gift is valid because there is no co-ownership according to them, just as in the gift of one house to two (from one). Likewise, charity is valid because charity to the rich is intended as a gift metaphorically, and a gift is permissible. And if one gives charity of ten to two poor people or gives a gift of ten to them, it is valid by consensus; because charity is intended for the sake of Allah, the Exalted. He ﷺ said: 'Charity falls into the hand of the Most Merciful before it falls into the hand of the beggar.' Do you not see that if he pays the debt of one of them, the whole remains in the hand of the other, unlike a gift, because ownership is not obtained for the donee except in half. [1] His saying: 'Like giving charity of ten...' etc.; and this is according to Abu Hanifa in the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, and according to the two companions it is valid for two rich people as well. Abu Hanifa on this narration differentiated between sadaqa: which is given to the poor even if expressed as a gift, and hiba: which is given to the rich even if expressed as sadaqa. He made them equal in invalidity in the original narration (riwayat al-asl) where he said after the gift issue: 'and likewise charity': i.e., it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa for two men, just as the gift is not permissible. The apparent view is that he has two narrations on this issue: the basis of the original narration is that the completion of charity is by possession, and each one encounters a co-owned item, so charity is not complete, as the gift is not complete. The basis of the narration of al-Jami' is that charity is intended for the sake of Allah the Exalted; for this reason, there is no recourse (ruju') in it; rather, it falls to the two poor by way of deputyship (niyaba), and Allah is one with no partner, so co-ownership does not occur in it; and it becomes like a gift if it occurs to one and two receive it by way of deputyship; while a gift is intended for two rich people, so co-ownership occurs in it.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of sadaqa (charity) given to multiple recipients, distinguishing between two rich people (aghniya') and two poor people (fuqara'). The matn states: giving ten dirhams to two rich people is not valid (according to Abu Hanifa), but giving to two poor people is valid by consensus. The commentary expands: At Abu Hanifa, giving to two rich is invalid in the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, while the two companions (Sahibayn) consider it valid. There is also an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: in the original narration (riwayat al-asl), Abu Hanifa equates sadaqa to hiba in both being invalid, while in al-Jami' he distinguishes: sadaqa to poor is valid because it is intended for Allah alone, and the poor receive as deputies (nuwwab) of Allah, so no co-ownership; hiba to rich is a direct transfer to two parties, causing co-ownership. The key proof is the hadith that charity falls into Allah's hand first, emphasizing the singular divine recipient. The distinction between sadaqa and hiba is context-dependent: sadaqa is for the poor seeking reward, hiba is for the rich as a pure donation.

Key Terms
صدقة (sadaqa): Charitable giving intended for the sake of Allah, typically to the poor; here distinguished from hiba when given to the rich.
نيابة (niyaba): Deputyship or representation; in this context, the poor recipients act as deputies for Allah in receiving charity.
رجوع (ruju'): Recourse or revocation; in sadaqa, unlike hiba, the donor cannot reclaim the gift because it is solely for Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because charity to the poor is for Allah, so the poor are deputies, avoiding co-ownership.
B) Because charity to the rich is considered a loan.
C) Because the poor are more deserving of co-ownership.
D) Because charity to two poor is invalid by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The validity of sadaqa to multiple recipients depends on whether they are deputies (poor) or direct recipients (rich); only the latter induces co-ownership (shuyu') which invalidates the transfer.

Hibah to the Poor is Sadaqah: Ruling and Intra-Madhhab Reconciliation
Defining the ruling of hibah when given to the poor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of hibah to the poor and explain why the condition of shuyu' is waived, and also explain the reconciliation of two Hanafi narrations on this issue.

Core Idea
A gift to the poor is treated as sadaqah, so it is valid even if undivided.

In Hanafi fiqh, gifts (hibah) normally require specification and division if the item is undivided (mushaa'). However, when the recipient is poor, the gift takes the ruling of sadaqah, which does not require such division. This lesson explores that ruling and an intra-Hanafi discussion that reconciles different narrations about the meaning of sadaqah in the original text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and recipient
The act is a gift (hibah) and the recipient is a poor person (faqir). Note the specific context.
2
Recognize the general rule of shuyu' in hibah
In ordinary hibah, if the item is undivided (mushaa'), the gift is invalid unless the recipient takes possession after division. This is a condition for validity.
Shuyu' refers to common ownership or undivided share.
3
Apply the exception for the poor recipient
The passage states: 'وأما الهبة على الفقير فهي صدقة' – the gift to the poor is sadaqah. Since sadaqah is permissible (jai'iz) without requiring division, the same applies to hibah when given to the poor.
The condition of shuyu' is waived.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab reconciliation
The text mentions a view (قيل) that the word 'sadaqah' in the original matn refers to sadaqah given to the rich, not the poor. This interpretation resolves an apparent contradiction between two narrations.
See the madhhab dispute map for details.
5
Note the practical consequence
Practically, a gift to a poor person does not require division of an undivided share; it is valid as long as the intention is genuine. This facilitates charity without strict formalities.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling segment from the Book of Gifts (Kitab al-Hibah) in a Hanafi commentary. It clarifies the relationship between hibah (gift) and sadaqah (charity), specifically when the recipient is poor. The author states that when a gift is given to a poor person, it is legally considered sadaqah, and since sadaqah is permissible, the gift is also permissible. The phrase 'فلا شيوع' indicates that the usual condition in hibah – that the gifted item must be divided or specified if it is undivided (shuyu') – is waived here because sadaqah rules apply, which do not require such division. Then the author mentions a statement (وقيل) that the sadaqah referred to in the original text (the matn) is actually sadaqah given to the rich, not to the poor. This interpretation resolves an apparent contradiction between two different narrations (روايتين) on the ruling. The passage ends with a reference to Kamal al-Dirayah, a commentary, and a marginal note 'بصدقة' which may be a correction or addition. The footnote provides a supporting hadith from Abu Hurairah about the virtue of sadaqah, reinforcing the merit of giving.

الهبة (al-hibah): A gift or donation, defined in the book title as the subject of this chapter.
صدقة (sadaqah): Charity given with the intention of seeking Allah's reward; here it is specifically used to describe a gift to the poor.
شيوع (shuyu'): Common ownership or undivided share; in the context, it refers to the condition in hibah that the gifted item must be specified and divided if shared.

Ruling on Revocation of a Gift with Proof
Hanafi ruling on gift revocation with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revocation of a gift and explain how two hadith are reconciled to produce the ruling of validity with prohibitive dislike.

Core Idea
Revocation of a gift is valid but prohibitively disliked, except for a father — a reconciliation of two hadith.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the commentator expands on the base text from Al-Hidayah regarding the revocation of a gift (ruju' fi al-hibah). The Hanafi school affirms the validity of revocation based on a hadith that the donor is more entitled to his gift until compensated, yet another hadith strongly prohibits revocation except for a father. The commentator harmonizes these proofs to establish that revocation is sahih (valid) but makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked). Understanding this reconciliation is key to grasping Hanafi methodology in dealing with apparently conflicting textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The revocation of a gift by the donor after possession. What is the ruling? The text states it is valid (sahih) but disliked (makruh). Identify the specific ruling category: validity is sahih, and the dislike is makruh tahriman (prohibitive) according to the relied-upon view.
The act itself is the donor taking back the gifted property from the donee.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith are cited. The first: 'al-wahib ahaqqu bihibbatihi' (the donor is more entitled to his gift) — this supports validity. The second: 'la yahillu li rajulin...' (it is not permissible except for a father) — this prohibits revocation except for a father. Identify the source and transmission status of each (both are ahad, zanni al-thubut).
The first hadith is narrated by Ibn Majah, al-Daraqutni, et al.; the second by Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, Ahmad.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does each hadith prove its point? The phrase 'ahaqqu' indicates a right or entitlement, implying permissibility. The condition 'ma lam yuthab' limits revocation to cases where compensation has not been given. The phrase 'la yahillu' indicates absolute prohibition, but the exception for the father shows it is not universal.
The Hanafi method: the second hadith's prohibition is interpreted as strong dislike (karahah tahrimiyyah) rather than nullity, to allow both proofs to operate.
4
Understand the reconciliation
The commentator reconciles by giving effect to both: the first hadith establishes that revocation is valid (sahih) in principle; the second establishes that doing so is severely disliked (makruh tahriman). Thus, revocation is legally possible but sinful, except for a father (one of the rare exceptions where it is not disliked).
This is a classic example of jam' (reconciliation) over tarjih (preference).
5
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school generally holds that revocation after possession is invalid because the gift contract is binding. The Hanafi school differs. Also, within the Hanafi school there is a difference on whether the dislike is tahrimi or tanzihi; the mufta bihi is makruh tahriman.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that a Hanafi donor may reclaim the gift, but a Shafi'i donor cannot, except for a father.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is an expansion on the ruling of revocation (ruju') in a gift (hibah). The base text states that if someone gives a gift and then revokes it, the revocation is valid (sahih). The commentary clarifies that this is the Hanafi position ('inda al-taa' — i.e., our school) and provides proof from hadith. The key point is that a gift, once delivered, transfers ownership to the donee. However, revocation is possible because the donor retains a right to reclaim the gift. The commentary then reconciles two hadith: one that prohibits revocation except for a father ('la yahillu li rajulin...') and another that says the donor is more entitled to his gift until compensated ('al-wahibu ahaqqu bihibbatihi'). The Hanafi school harmonizes them by allowing revocation (based on the second hadith) but declaring it severely disliked (makruh tahriman) according to the first. A minority view considers it merely disfavored (makruh tanzihan). The passage also cites the sources of the second hadith from Ibn Majah, al-Daraqutni, Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Hakim, and al-Tabarani. Thus, the ruling is: revocation of a completed gift is valid but is prohibitively disliked, except for a father revoking a gift given to his child, which is permissible without dislike.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Revocation of a gift; the act of the donor taking back the gifted property from the donee after possession.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; a contract whereby a person transfers ownership of property to another without compensation.
الواهب (al-wahib): The donor; the person who gives the gift.
كراهة التحريم (karāhat al-taḥrīm): Prohibitive dislike; a ruling close to haram, but not as strong. The act is sinful but not invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, what is the ruling on a donor revoking a gift after it has been given and taken possession of?
A) The revocation is invalid and the gift remains with the donee.
B) The revocation is valid but severely disliked (makruh tahriman), except for a father revoking from his child.
C) The revocation is valid and recommended.
D) The revocation is valid only if the donor returns the compensation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on gift revocation is valid but prohibitively disliked, achieved by reconciling two hadith rather than discarding one.

Revocation of Gifts: Validity vs. Dislike
Stating the Hanafi ruling and reconciling conflicting hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gift revocation, identify the two conflicting hadith proofs, and explain the Hanafi reconciliation through the karahah category.

Core Idea
A gift can be revoked legally but not ethically – validity and desirability are distinct.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses the issue of revoking a gift after possession. The Hanafi school affirms the legal validity of revocation while acknowledging that it is morally disliked. Understanding this requires reconciling two hadith that seem to contradict each other. The passage also presents the Shafi'i counter-position and the Hanafi rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What exactly does the text rule regarding revocation? It states 'صح' (valid) but then mentions karahah (dislike). So the hukm is that revocation is legally valid (sahih) but disliked (makruh).
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited: 'الرجل أحق بهبته ما لم يثب منها' (The man has more right to his gift as long as he has not been compensated). This establishes the donor's right to revoke before compensation.
The word 'أحق' indicates a superior right, implying permissibility.
3
Identify the opposing dalil
Find the hadith forbidding revocation: 'لا يحل لرجل... إلا الوالد فيما يعطي ولده' (It is not lawful for a man... except a parent regarding what he gives his child). This seems to prohibit revocation entirely except for parents.
This hadith is also authentic and must be reconciled.
4
Understand the Hanafi reconciliation
The Hanafis interpret the forbidding hadith as indicating karahah (dislike) rather than tahrim (prohibition). Thus revocation is valid but disliked. This harmonizes both proofs.
The commentator notes a difference of opinion whether the karahah is tahrimi (near-forbidden) or tanzihi (off-putting).
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: A Hanafi can revoke a gift (though disliked), while a Shafi'i cannot revoke except if the donor is the father. This affects daily transactions and court rulings.

Chapter on revocation of gifts. Whoever gives a gift and then revokes it, it is valid. This is according to us (Hanafis) because of his saying: 'The donor has more right to his gift as long as he has not been compensated.' The meaning of 'as long as he has not been compensated' is as long as he has not received a return gift. According to al-Shafi'i, revocation is not valid except in the gift of a parent to his child. And it is reported by 'Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf from Sufyan from Mansur from Ibrahim that 'Umar said: 'Whoever gives a gift to a relative has no right to revoke it, and whoever gives to a non-relative has the right to revoke it unless he receives a return gift.' This is the proof by transmission. As for proof by reason, the purpose of a gift is usually compensation and recompense. Do you not see that it is said: 'Favors are loans.' And this is supported by his saying: 'Exchange gifts so that you love each other,' for the mutual form (tafa'ul) implies action from both sides. So he has the right to revoke if his purpose is not achieved, just as a buyer who finds a defect in the goods returns the price because his purpose (the condition of soundness) is frustrated. Thus, the donor has the right to rescind when his purpose is frustrated, because the contract accepts it.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the chapter of revocation of gifts. It begins by stating the ruling: if a person gives a gift and then revokes it, the revocation is valid according to the Hanafi school. The commentator explains that this is because the gift does not become fully binding until compensation (ithabah) occurs. The proof is a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ: 'The donor has more right to his gift as long as he has not been compensated.' However, there is another hadith forbidding revocation except for a parent giving to his child, comparing the revoker to a dog that vomits and returns to its vomit. To reconcile these, the Hanafis say that revocation is legally valid but disliked (makruh tahriman or tanzihan). The passage then notes the Shafi'i position that revocation is only valid in the case of a parent's gift to his child, and mentions that Malik and Ahmad also hold this view in the apparent position of their schools. There is also a rational argument (istidlal bi al-dirayah) that gifts are usually given with the expectation of reciprocation, and the donor's purpose is frustrated if no compensation is received. This is analogized to the buyer's right of return due to a defect. Thus, the Hanafi position is that revocation is permissible except when prevented by a specific prohibition, such as a gift to a relative or after compensation.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Revocation of a gift after it has been given and possessed.
الإثابة (al-ithabah): Compensation or return gift given by the recipient to the donor.
الكراهة (al-karahah): Dislike; the text mentions it as karaahat al-tahrim (disliked as forbidden) or tanzih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A gift can be revoked legally but not ethically; the Hanafi school reconciles conflicting hadith through the category of karahah.

Revocation of Gifts: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi proof and response to Shafi'i on gift revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on revocation of gifts, evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two schools.

Core Idea
A gift to a non-relative is revocable unless compensated; to a relative, it is not; the Shafi'i hadith is a moral guide, not a legal bar.

This lesson tackles a classical inter-madhhab dispute over whether a donor may revoke a gift (hiba) when no compensation has been given. The Hanafi school, relying on a narration from 'Umar and rational analogy, permits revocation for non-relative gifts unless compensated. The Shafi'i school, citing a specific hadith, prohibits revocation except for a parent. The Hanafi response reinterprets the hadith as a moral recommendation, not a legal prohibition, and the parent's right is justified by a separate principle.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the three categories: (1) gift to a relative – irrevocable; (2) gift to a non-relative without compensation – revocable; (3) gift from a parent to a child – revocable due to father's special right.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that revocation is not permissible except for a parent giving to his child, based on the hadith: 'The donor may not revoke a gift except a parent in what he gives to his child.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i reliance on this explicit hadith. Understand its apparent generality ('لا يرجع الواهب' – the donor does not return).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith as a moral recommendation (diyana/muru'a), not a legal prohibition (qada'). They support this by analogy: the hadith 'لا يحل لرجل... أن يبيت شبعان' is similarly moral, yet Abu Hanifa allowed it legally. Also, the father's right is based on 'You and your wealth belong to your father.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In a case where a non-parent gives to a non-relative without compensation, the Hanafi school allows legal revocation; the Shafi'i school does not. For a parent giving to a child, both agree revocation is allowed, but the basis differs.

ما لم يثبت ) : أي ما لم يعوض ، وعند الشافعي لا تصح إلا في هبة الوالد لولده. روى عبد الرزاق في مصنفه: عن سفيان عن منصور عن ابراهيم قال: قال عمر: «من وهب هبة لذي رحم فليس له أن يرجع فيها، ومن وهب لغير ذي رحم فله أن يرجع فيها إلا أن يثاب منها». هذا هو الاستدلال بالرواية. أما الاستدلال بالدراية فهو أنَّ المقصود بالهبة هو التعويض والمكافأة عادة، ألا ترى أنه يقال: الأيادي قروض وتأيد ذلك بقوله: «تهادوا تحابوا»، فإنّ التفاعل يقتضي الفعل من الجانبين. فكان له الرجوع إذا لم يحصل مقصوده، كما أنّ المشتري إذا وجد بالمبيع عيباً يرجع بالثمن لفوات مقصوده وهو وصف السلامة في المبيع، فيثبت للواهب ولاية الفسخ عند فوات مقصوده، إذ العقد يقبله. لقوله: «لا يرجعُ الواهب في هبة إلا الوالد فيما يهب لولده»، ونحن نقول به: أي لا ينبغي أن يرجع في هبة إلا الوالد. لقوله: هذا استدلال بالرواية، وأما الاستدلال بالدراية فهو أنَّ الرجوع يضاد مقتضى العقد، وهو التمليك، والعقد لا يقتضي ما يضاده. ولا يقال: إن هذه العلة موجودة في هبة الوالد للولد أيضاً، فيلزمه أن لا يصح الرجوع فيها أيضاً؛ لأنا نقول: جواز الرجوع في هبة الوالد لولده على أصله له، فإنَّ من أصله أن للأب حق في مال ابنه؛ لأنه جزؤه أو كسبه، فالتمليك من نفسه من وجه. ونحن نقول... هذا جواب عمّا استدل به الشافعي له من الحديث، وتقريره: إنّ المراد بما رواه عدم استقلال الواهب بالرجوع من غير قضاء ولا رضاء، إلا الوالد إذا احتاج إلى ذلك، فإنّه ينفرد بالأخذ لحاجته بلا رضاء ولا قضاء، كما في سائر أمواله، بناءً على ما قال رسول الله: أنت ومالك لأبيك، إنَّ أطيب ما أكلتم من كسبكم، وإن أولادكم من كسبكم، فكلوه هنياً. وعلى أن هذا الحديث الذي رواه واستدل به لا ينافي الرجوع، فإنَّ المراد به أن الواهب لا يحل له الرجوعُ ديانة ومروءة، لا أنه لا يحلُ قضاء وحكماً، كما قال: «لا يحل لرجل يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر أن يبيت شبعان»، وأجازه أبو حنيفة له طارئاً، فمعناه لا يليق به مروءة وديانة، وإن كان جائزاً قضاء وحكماً.
Unless compensated: i.e., unless recompensed. According to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid except in the gift of a parent to his child. Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his Musannaf: from Sufyan from Mansur from Ibrahim, he said: 'Umar said: 'Whoever gives a gift to a relative has no right to revoke it, and whoever gives to a non-relative has the right to revoke it unless he is recompensed from it.' This is the textual proof. As for the rational proof, it is that the purpose of a gift is normally compensation and reciprocation; do you not see that it is said: 'Favors are loans,' and this is supported by his statement: 'Exchange gifts, for you will love each other,' for the mutual interaction (tafa'ul) requires action from both sides. So he may revoke when his purpose is not achieved, just as a buyer who finds a defect in the purchased item may return the price for loss of his purpose, which is the description of soundness in the item. Thus the donor has the authority to rescind when his purpose is lost, since the contract accepts it. For his statement: 'The donor may not revoke a gift except a parent in what he gives to his child,' and we accept it: i.e., it is not proper for anyone to revoke a gift except a parent. For his statement: this is textual proof. As for the rational proof, it is that revocation contradicts the requirement of the contract, which is ownership, and the contract does not require its opposite. It cannot be said that this cause exists also in the parent's gift to his child, so that revocation should not be valid there as well; because we say: The permissibility of revocation in a parent's gift to his child is based on his principle, for it is part of his principle that the father has a right over his child's property because the child is a part of him or his earning, so the transfer of ownership is from himself in a sense. And we say... this is a response to al-Shafi'i's proof from the hadith, and its explanation: The meaning of what he narrated is that the donor does not have independent authority to revoke without court judgment or consent, except the parent if he needs it, for he may take it independently without consent or judgment, as in his other property, based on the Prophet's statement: 'You and your wealth belong to your father; indeed the best of what you eat is from your earnings, and indeed your children are from your earnings, so eat from it with enjoyment.' And also that this hadith which he narrated as proof does not negate revocation, for its meaning is that revocation is not permissible for the donor as a matter of piety and chivalry, not that it is not permissible as a matter of law and judgment, just as it is said: 'It is not permissible for a man who believes in Allah and the Last Day to go to bed full,' and Abu Hanifa permitted it for a sudden guest, so its meaning is that it is not befitting as chivalry and piety, even though it is legally permissible as a matter of law and judgment.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal ruling on revoking a gift (hiba). The Hanafi school, citing a narration from 'Umar (radiyallahu 'anhu), holds that a gift given to a relative (dhi rahm) cannot be revoked, but a gift given to a non-relative can be revoked unless the donor has received compensation (ithaba). The text presents both a textual proof (riwaya) and a rational proof (diraya). The rational proof analogizes a gift to a sale: just as a buyer may return a defective item because his purpose (a sound item) was not achieved, so too a donor may revoke if he does not receive the expected reciprocation. This is supported by the principle that 'favors are loans' and the hadith 'Exchange gifts, for you will love each other,' which implies mutual action. The Shafi'i objection is based on the explicit hadith: 'The donor may not revoke a gift except a parent in what he gives to his child.' The Hanafi response interprets this hadith as a moral recommendation (diyana/ muru'a) rather than a legal prohibition. Thus legally, revocation is permissible, but it is not befitting one's dignity except for a parent. Furthermore, the parent's unique right to take from his child's property is established by the hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' Therefore, the Hanafi position distinguishes between the legal ruling (qada') and the moral ruling (diyana), allowing revocation legally but discouraging it ethically except for parents.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Revocation or rescission of a gift by the donor; taking back the gifted property.
الإثابة (al-ithaba): Compensation or recompense given by the donee to the donor in return for the gift.
الاستدلال بالدراية (al-istidlal bi al-diraya): Rational proof based on legal reasoning and analogy, as opposed to textual proof (riwaya).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, may a person revoke a gift given to a non-relative if the recipient has not given any compensation?
A) Yes, revocation is legally permissible.
B) No, revocation is never allowed.
C) Yes, but only if the donor is the parent.
D) No, because the hadith prohibits revocation for all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a gift to a non-relative is revocable if uncompensated; a gift to a relative is irrevocable; and a parent's gift to a child is revocable based on the father's right over the child's property.

Attached Increase and Its Effect on Gift Revocation
Commentary on prohibition of revocation after attached increase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a connected increase in the gifted item prevents revocation, identify the ten impediments to revocation from the text, and apply the distinction between attached increase and price increase.

Core Idea
A connected increase in the gifted item bars revocation; a mere price rise does not.

This passage tackles a practical question: once you have given a gift, can you take it back? The Hanafi school generally allows revocation, but only if the gift remains exactly as it was. If the donee has added something attached to it—like building on land, dyeing cloth, or teaching a slave—the donor loses the right to revoke. The passage explains the underlying reasoning, lists ten specific impediments, and resolves internal disputes within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
State the core rule: 'A connected increase prevents revocation.' Then locate the hadith analogy used to support it. Note how the commentator compares revoking after an increase to a believer committing adultery or lying—both are inherently incompatible with the act's virtue.
The hadith is used as qiyas (analogy), not direct proof.
2
List the ten impediments (mawani')
Extract the ten cases from the passage where revocation is prevented. Group them: death of parties, increase (by donee or in the gift itself), teaching a skill, receiving compensation, destruction, moving with transport cost, building on land, alienation, and spousal gifts.
The list is not exhaustive but covers common scenarios.
3
Define 'connected increase' precisely
The passage distinguishes between an increase in the entity (ayn) and a mere increase in value. Only an attached increase—like building, dyeing, tailoring, or teaching that becomes part of the slave—bars revocation. Growth in height or a rise in market price does not count.
The key is whether the increase is physically or effectively inseparable.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi disputes
Examine two specific disputes: (a) moving the item with transport cost (Abu Yusuf differs), and (b) teaching the slave (Zufar vs. the majority, with Qadi Khan's view prevailing). Understand the reasoning behind each position and note which is mufta bihi.
The fatwa relies on Qadi Khan's opinion that teaching creates an increase in the entity.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., donee plants trees on gifted land; land value increases; donor wants to revoke), determine whether revocation is allowed. Use the criteria: is the increase attached to the entity? If yes, revocation is barred; if only price increase, allowed.
Remember: the donor's word is taken if dispute arises about the increase.

The intended meaning of increase is increase in the gifted item itself by something that causes an increase in value, as mentioned in the Matn, and like beautification, tailoring, dyeing, and the like. Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a connected increase (ziyada muttasila) in a gifted item prevents the donor from revoking (ruju') the gift. The author begins by supporting the ruling with a hadith analogy: just as lying and adultery are incompatible with faith, so too revoking a gift after an increase is incompatible with the generosity expected from a believer. The commentary then explains that the increase must be in the substance of the gifted item, not merely in its market value. To illustrate, the text lists ten impediments (mawani') to revocation: death of donor or donee, increase by the donee or in the gift itself, teaching the slave a skill, receiving compensation, destruction of the gift, moving the item with transport cost (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, not Abu Yusuf), building on land (if it increases value), alienation of the gift, and spousal gifts. The passage then delves deeper into what constitutes an attached increase. If the increase is only in price (e.g., due to market demand), revocation is still allowed because the entity itself hasn't changed. Physical growth like a boy growing taller is considered a decrease in terms of usability, so it does not bar revocation. There is a dispute regarding teaching the slave: the relied-upon position (from Qadi Khan) is that teaching the Quran, writing, or speech makes the gift irrevocable because it creates a real increase in the entity. The commentary also notes that if there is a dispute about the occurrence of an increase, the donor's word is taken because he denies the validity of the contract of gift.

Key Terms
الزيادة المتصلة (al-ziyada al-muttasila): An increase that is physically attached to the gifted item (e.g., building, dyeing, tailoring) and increases its value, as opposed to a disconnected increase (e.g., fruit) or a mere price increase.
الرجوع في الهبة (al-ruju' fi al-hibah): The act of the donor taking back the gifted item after the gift is complete.
الموانع (al-mawani'): The impediments that prevent the donor from revoking the gift. The text lists ten.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a gifted slave is taught the Quran by the donee, what is the ruling on the donor's right to revoke the gift?
A) The donor may revoke because teaching is not an increase in the entity.
B) The donor cannot revoke according to Qadi Khan's fatwa because teaching creates an increase in the entity.
C) The donor can only revoke if he pays compensation for the teaching.
D) The donee must consent to the revocation.

Key Takeaway
A connected increase in the gifted item bars revocation; teaching a slave is treated as such an increase in the mufta bihi position.

Conditions Preventing Revocation of Gift: Connected Increases and Death
Exposition of impediments to revocation of gift: connected increases and death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disconnected increases and state which impediments prevent the donor from revoking a gift in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Only physical increases attached to the gifted property—and death of either party—prevent revocation; learning and market changes do not.

Revocation of a gift (rujū') is generally permissible in Hanafi law, but certain conditions render it irrevocable. The most critical distinction is whether an increase in the gifted property is physically connected (muttaṣil) or separate (munfaṣil). Connected increases, such as building, planting, or dyeing, permanently block revocation because they become part of the substance. Death of the donor or donee also terminates the right of revocation. This passage from a super-commentary on al-Wiqāyah explores these rules and addresses internal disagreements within the Hanafi school, particularly regarding teaching a slave and moving property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its qualifications
Determine the general ruling: revocation of a gift is permissible (jā'iz) unless impeded by one of the specified conditions. The passage lists several impediments: connected increase, death, and certain events like conversion to Islam.
The burden is on the donor to show that no impediment exists.
2
Distinguish connected vs. disconnected increase
Examine the text's criterion: only increases that alter the physical substance (e.g., building, planting, fattening, dyeing) prevent revocation. Disconnected increases like offspring, rent, or compensation do not. Market value increases are also irrelevant.
Connected increase must be in the 'ayn (substance), not merely in value.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreements
The passage records disputes: (a) Moving property with transport expenses prevents revocation according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf alone differs (as per one view). (b) Teaching the slave the Quran, writing, or a craft does not prevent revocation per the majority, but Zufar and Qādī Khān's Fatāwā consider it an increase in substance.
These disagreements affect the practical outcome when a donor seeks to revoke an enhanced slave.
4
Apply the rule to death of a party
Death of the donor or donee permanently prevents revocation. The donor's option is a personal right that does not transfer to heirs; after donee's death, ownership passes from the donee, not from the donor.
This is analogous to options (khiyārāt) that are not inherited.
5
Consider reversal of impediment
Note that if the donee removes the connected increase (e.g., demolishes a building), the impediment vanishes and revocation becomes permissible again.
The right of revocation is suspended, not permanently extinguished.

The Book of Gift: Chapter on Revocation Therein. Like a building [1]: An increase in the gifted thing itself that produces an increase in value, as mentioned in the Matn, and like beauty, tailoring, dyeing, and the like. And if it increased in price, he has the right to revoke, because there is no increase in the substance. Similarly, if it increased in itself without increasing in value, like when the gifted slave boy grows tall, it is actually a decrease, so it does not prevent revocation. And if he moved it from place to place until its value increased and it required transportation expenses, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the right of revocation is cut off, contrary to Abu Yusuf. If he gifted an infidel slave, then he embraced Islam in the hands of the donee, or gifted a slave whose blood was lawful (due to a crime), then the guardian of the crime pardoned while the slave was in the hands of the donee, he does not revoke. And if the crime was unintentional (khatā') and the donee ransomed him, it does not prevent revocation. (1)4 And if the donee taught the slave the Quran, writing, or a craft, it does not prevent revocation, because these are not an increase in the substance. There is disagreement with Zufar in this, and a different version of the disagreement is narrated, as mentioned by al-'Ayni, al-Zayla'i, and others. However, Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: If he taught him the Quran, writing, or reading, or (the slave) was a foreigner and he taught him speech or something of letters, the donor cannot revoke his gift because an increase has occurred in the substance. End quote. If they disagree about the increase, the word is that of the donor, because he denies the binding nature of the contract. And it (the increase) is qualified as preventing revocation only if it is connected, because the disconnected does not prevent it, as the commentator also mentioned. And we interpreted increase as being in the gifted thing itself, because if it were in its value it does not prevent, because then it is due to people's desire; the substance remains as it was, so it does not prevent revocation. [1] His words: like a building; on the land if it causes an increase in it, but if it does not cause an increase, then no (it does not prevent), unless the donee consumes it. 9. If he removes it from his ownership by sale, gift, or charity. 10. Gift of a woman to her husband and gift of a husband to his wife. See al-Nutaf (1:515-516). (1) In Ramz al-Haqa'iq (2:187-188). (2) In al-Tabyin (5:98-99). And planting, fattening, not the disconnected, and death of one of the two contracting parties. And planting" [1] Zubdat al-Nihayah li 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah [2], and fattening, not the disconnected), which is like offspring, and death of one of the two contracting parties (2) (1) (2) prevent revocation. But if it causes an increase in a portion of it, as when the land is large such that the like of it is not considered an increase in the whole, then it prevents only in that portion, not in the rest, as mentioned by al-'Ayni, al-Zayla'i, and others. And it is said in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyyah: If he gifted land and the donee built a building on it, revocation is invalidated, and if the building disappears the right of revocation returns. End quote. [1] His words: and planting; meaning planting trees in the gifted land, for if the donor gave a white (unimproved) land and the donee planted in one of its sides or built a house, shop, or stable, and that was an increase in it, then he cannot revoke anything from it, because this is a connected increase. As in al-Hidayah (3). Then if the donee demolishes the increase and uproots the trees and the land returns as it was, the donor may revoke due to the removal of the impediment. As in al-Kifayah. [1] His words: not the disconnected; which is like offspring, meaning the disconnected increase does not prevent revocation, like offspring, rent (profit), and compensation for defects (arsh). He revokes the original without the increase, because separation is possible, contrary to return due to defect (radd bi al-'ayb), where it is prevented by the existence of offspring, because it is a commutative contract. If he returned the original without the increase, it would lead to riba because the offspring would be taken for free, and returning the offspring with it is not possible because the contract is returned upon it, so it (the return) becomes invalid entirely, and he receives compensation for the defect. [2] His words: and death of one of the two contracting parties; meaning the death of one of the two, the donor or the donee, prevents revocation, because with the death of the donor his option becomes void, as it is a personal right (description) that is not inherited, like the option of inspection and of stipulation; and with the death of the donee, ownership is transferred to his heirs, and they did not obtain it from the donor, so the donor cannot revoke against them. It is as if it had been transferred to them during his lifetime. And also, the change of ownership is like the change of the substance, so it becomes like another substance. And when the donor dies, his heirs are bound by the contract, as it is what he obligated, and the right of revocation
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on revocation of gifts in the Hanafi school, specifically from a super-commentary (hashiya) on al-Wiqayah. It addresses the conditions that prevent the donor from revoking a gift. The central issue is the nature of increases (ziyāda) in the gifted property and other impediments like death of a party.

The passage first distinguishes between increases in the substance of the gifted item itself and increases in its value due to market factors. Only the former prevents revocation. Examples of substance increases include building, beautification, tailoring, and dyeing—these add a physical change that enhances value. A mere rise in price due to supply and demand does not prevent revocation, because the substance remains unchanged. Similarly, an increase in the substance that does not increase value, such as a slave growing taller (which is actually a natural process that often indicates aging and decline), does not prevent revocation; in fact, it is considered a deficiency.

The text then addresses a scenario where the donor moves the gifted property to another location, increasing its value but incurring transportation costs. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two preeminent disciples—Sāhibayn), this cuts off the right of revocation, because the move is a substantive change. Abu Yūsuf alone disagrees, holding that relocation without an actual change in the substance does not prevent revocation.

Next, the passage discusses cases where the gifted slave converts to Islam or a crime is pardoned. If the slave accepts Islam while in the donee's possession, the donor cannot revoke, because Islam is a higher state and a blessing that cannot be undone. Similarly, if the donor gifted a slave whose blood was lawful (i.e., a murderer) and the guardian pardons, revocation is prevented. However, if the crime was unintentional (khaṭā') and the donee pays the blood money (ransom), revocation is not prevented—the ransom is not an increase in the slave's substance.

Teaching the slave the Quran, writing, or a craft is a notable point of debate. The passage states that according to the general opinion (including Imām al-Shāfiʿī?), these do not prevent revocation because they are not physical increases. However, Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) held that they do prevent revocation, and there is a conflicting narration. Qāḍī Khān in his Fatāwā supports Zufar's view, claiming that such education creates an increase in the substance (cognitive enhancement). The passage then notes that in case of disagreement about whether an increase occurred, the donor's word is taken because he denies the binding nature of the gift.

The text then defines the key qualifier: only connected (muttasil) increases prevent revocation. Disconnected (munfaṣil) increases, such as offspring, rent, or compensation for defects, do not. The reasoning is that revocation can be made on the original while leaving these disconnected elements separate, unlike the return of a defective item (radd bi-al-ʿayb) in a sale contract where the increase must be returned with the item to avoid riba.

Finally, death of either the donor or the donee permanently prevents revocation. The donor's option is a personal right that does not transfer to heirs; upon his death, the gift becomes binding. Upon the donee's death, ownership passes to his heirs from the donee, not from the donor, so the donor cannot revoke against them.

A subtle point: if the donee later removes the increase (e.g., demolishes a building), the impediment is removed and the donor may revoke. Also, if the gifted land is large and only part of it is improved, revocation is only prevented for that part.

Key Terms
الزيادة المتصلة (al-ziyāda al-muttaṣila): An increase that is physically attached to the gifted property, such as a building, planting, fattening, or dyeing.
الزيادة المنفصلة (al-ziyāda al-munfaṣila): An increase that is separate from the gifted property, such as offspring, rent, or compensation for defects.
الرجوع (al-rujū'): The act of the donor revoking or taking back a gift that he had given.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A donor gives a piece of land to a donee. The donee builds a house on it. Later, the donee demolishes the house and returns the land to its original state. Which of the following is correct?
A) The donor may now revoke the gift because the impediment has been removed.
B) The donor still cannot revoke because the house was once built.
C) The donor can revoke only if the donee agrees.
D) The donor cannot revoke because the land is now considered a different substance.

Key Takeaway
Only physical, connected increases and death of a party permanently prevent revocation of a gift in Hanafi law; teaching and market price changes do not.

Connected Increase Prevents Revocation of Gift
Explains that connected increase prevents revocation of gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a connected increase prevents gift revocation and identify the exception when revocation is restored.

Core Idea
A connected increase blocks the donor's right to revoke; removal of the increase restores that right.

When a gift is made, the donor generally has the right to revoke under certain conditions. However, if the donee adds a physically connected increase to the gifted property, such as planting trees or constructing a building, that right is lost. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on connected increases and clarifies when the donor regains the right to revoke.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the addition of a connected increase to the gifted property. The ruling is that the donor cannot revoke any part of the gift.
2
Understand the reasoning
The increase is inseparable; to revoke would cause damage or injustice. The basis is the legal maxim that connected increase prevents revocation.
This is established in the Hanafi school.
3
Recognize the exception
If the donee removes the increase (e.g., demolishes the building, uproots trees), the barrier is removed and the donor may revoke.
This restoration of the right depends on the gift returning to its original state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغرس [۱]قوله : وغرس ؛ أي غرس الشجرَ في الأرض الموهوبة، فإن وهب الآخر أرضاً بيضاء فأنبت في ناحية منها أو بنى بيتاً أو دكانا أو آريا، وكان ذلك زيادة فيها فليس له أن يرجع في شيء منها ؛ لأنَّ هذه زيادة متصلة. كذا في «الهداية) (۳). ثم الموهوب له إن هدم الزيادة، وقلع الشجر وعادت كما كان للواهب أن يرجع لزوال المانع. كذا في «الكفاية).
And planting: meaning planting trees in the gifted land. So if one gives a barren land and the recipient plants in a part of it or builds a house or shop or stable, and that is an increase in it, then he does not have the right to revoke anything from it because this is a connected increase. This is stated in al-Hidayah. Then if the recipient demolishes the increase and uproots the trees and it returns as it was, the donor has the right to revoke due to the removal of the barrier. This is stated in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling regarding connected increase (ziyadah muttasila) in the context of gift revocation. The commentator clarifies that when the donee adds a physically attached increase such as planting trees or constructing a building on the gifted land, the donor loses the right to revoke the gift in its entirety because the increase cannot be separated without damage. This is based on the principle that connected increase prevents revocation. However, if the donee later removes the increase (e.g., demolishes the building or uproots the trees), the barrier is removed and the donor regains the right to revoke the original gift. This ruling is recorded in the authoritative Hanafi texts al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah.

Key Terms
الزيادة المتصلة (al-ziyādah al-muttaṣilah): An increase that is physically attached to the gifted property, such as planting trees or constructing a building.
الرجوع (ar-rujūʿ): The donor's right to take back the gifted property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) As soon as the increase is removed by the donee.
C) Only if the donee sells the property.
D) Only if the increase is damaged.

Key Takeaway
A connected increase blocks the donor's right to revoke a gift; removing the increase restores that right.

Disconnected Increase and Gift Revocation
Explains disconnected increase does not prevent revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a disconnected increase does not prevent revocation of a gift in the Hanafi school and distinguish it from the case of return for defect in sale.

Core Idea
Disconnected increase can be separated; it does not block gift revocation, unlike sale where separation would cause riba.

In Hanafi gift law, a connected increase (e.g., fattening, building) prevents revocation. But what about a child born after the gift, or dower received from a gifted slave? This passage clarifies that disconnected increases (al-ziyādah al-munfaṣilah) – those not physically attached – do not block revocation because they can be separated without affecting the original. The commentator contrasts this with the rule in sales: returning a defective item with a child is blocked because separating the child would lead to riba (unearned gain).

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of increase
Recognize the two categories: connected (muttasilah) and disconnected (munfasilah). Disconnected examples include a child, dower, and compensation for defect.
Connected increases are physically attached, like weight gain or building.
2
Understand the ruling for gifts
The donor can revoke the original gift and the donee keeps the disconnected increase. Separation is possible (imkān al-faṣl).
This applies to all disconnected increases.
3
Analyze the proof by contrast
Contrast with the sale case: if a defective item is returned after a child is born, returning only the original without the child would give the buyer the child for free (riba). So return is prevented entirely, and the buyer claims compensation instead.
The key difference: gift is not a commutative contract (muʿāwaḍah), so riba concern does not arise.
4
Apply the principle
In practice, if a gifted animal gives birth, the donor takes back the mother; the donee keeps the offspring. No compensation needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لا المنفصلة ؛ وهي مثل الولد ،والعقر ،والأرش، فإنّه يرجعُ في الأصل دون الزيادة ؛ لإمكان الفصل بخلاف الردّ بالعيب، حيث يمتنع بزيادة الولد ؛ لأنه عقد معاوضة. فلورد الأصل دون الزيادة لأدّى إلى الربا لسلامة الولد مجاناً، وردّ الولد معها لا يمكن ؛ لأن العقد يردّ عليه، فبطل أصلاً، ورجع بالنقصان.
His statement: 'not the disconnected [increase]' — meaning like a child, dower, and compensation for defect. So he revokes the original without the increase because separation is possible, unlike the return due to defect where it is prevented by the increase of a child because it is a commutative contract. So if the original is returned without the increase, it would lead to riba due to the child being obtained for free, and returning the child with it is not possible because the contract is upon it, so it becomes invalid entirely, and he takes back the amount of the defect.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between connected and disconnected increase in the context of gift revocation. Disconnected increase (munfasila) refers to a child born from a gifted animal, the dower ('aqr) of a gifted female slave, or compensation for defect (arsh). Such increase does not prevent the donor from revoking the original gift because it can be separated. The commentator contrasts this with the case of returning a defective item in a sale, where the birth of a child prevents return of the item. In sale, if the original item were returned without the child, the child would be obtained for free, constituting riba. In gift, since it is not a commutative contract, this concern does not apply. Therefore, in gift revocation, the donor takes back the original property and the donee keeps the disconnected increase.

Key Terms
الزيادة المنفصلة (al-ziyādah al-munfaṣilah): An increase that is not physically attached and can be separated, such as a child or dower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it cannot be separated from the original property.
B) Because separation would lead to riba.
C) Because it can be separated without harming the original.
D) Because the donee loses ownership of the increase.

Key Takeaway
A disconnected increase does not block gift revocation because it can be separated; this contrasts with sales where separation would cause riba.

Death of Either Party Prevents Revocation
Explains that death of donor or donee prevents revocation of gift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revocation after death of a party and explain the legal reasoning using the analogy of khiyar and change of ownership.

Core Idea
Death ends the donor's option to revoke; the right is personal and not inherited, like khiyar al-shart.

This lesson examines a key issue in Hanafi gift law: whether the right to revoke a gift (hiba) survives the death of either the donor or the donee. The source passage provides the Hanafi position with rational proofs, establishing that the option to revoke is a personal right that ceases upon death and does not pass to heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage clearly states that death of either the donor or the donee prevents revocation. This is the operative ruling.
This applies only after the gift has been delivered (taslim).
2
Analyse the donor's death scenario
When the donor dies, his option to revoke becomes void because it is a personal attribute (khiyar) that is not inherited, analogously to khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart.
The option is attached to the donor, not to the property.
3
Analyse the donee's death scenario
When the donee dies, ownership passes to his heirs by inheritance; they did not acquire it from the donor directly. Thus the donor cannot revoke from them, as the property is considered a different entity in their hands.
Change of ownership is like change of substance.
4
Apply the condition of delivery
The ruling is conditional on the gift having been delivered. Before delivery, the gift is invalid due to lack of ownership (the donee never took possession), so no revocation is needed.
This is explicitly stated at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وموت أحد المتعاقدين ؛ أي ويمنع الرجوع موتُ أحد المتعاقدين من الواهب والموهوب له ؛ لأنَّ بموتِ الواهب يبطل خياره ؛ لأنه وصف له، وهو لا يورث كخيار الرؤية والشرط، وبموتِ الموهوب انتقل الملك إلى ورثته، وهم لم يستفيدوه من جهة الواهب، فلا يرجع الواهب عليهم. كما إذا انتقل إليهم في حالِ حياته، ولأنَّ تبدّل الملك كتبدل العين، فصار كعين أخرى، وإذا بات الواهب فوارتُه أ عن العقد، إذ هو ما أوجبه، وحق الرجوع مجرد خيار كخيار الشرط فلا يورث، وهذا إذا كان بعد التسليم ؛ لأنه قبل التسليم بطلت؛ لعدم الملك. كما صرح به صاحب «المنح»)، وغيره.
His statement: 'and death of one of the two contracting parties' — meaning death of either the donor or the donee prevents revocation. Because with the death of the donor, his option becomes void since it is an attribute of him and is not inherited like the option of inspection and condition. With the death of the donee, ownership transfers to his heirs, and they did not acquire it from the donor, so the donor does not revoke from them. This is as if it transferred to them during his lifetime. And because change of ownership is like change of substance, so it becomes like another entity. And if the donor dies, his inheritors are removed from the contract because the contract is what he himself made, and the right of revocation is merely an option like the option of condition, so it is not inherited. This applies after delivery; before delivery the gift is invalid due to lack of ownership. This is as stated by the author of al-Minh and others.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage explains that death of either the donor or the donee nullifies the donor's right to revoke the gift. The reasoning is that the right to revoke is a personal option (khiyar) attached to the donor; upon his death, this option ceases and does not pass to his heirs, similar to the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) or condition (khiyar al-shart). If the donee dies, ownership has already transferred to his heirs by inheritance, not by the gift, so the donor cannot revoke from them because the property is now considered a different entity in their hands. This ruling applies only after the gift has been delivered (taslim); before delivery, the gift is invalid because the donee has not yet taken possession. This is the Hanafi position as recorded in al-Minh and other works.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyār): The option or right to revoke a contract; in this context, the donor's right to revoke the gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the donor's right to revoke a gift if the donee dies?
A) The donor can still revoke from the donee's heirs.
B) The right is transferred to the donor's heirs.
C) The right is lost because ownership has passed to heirs, making it a different entity.
D) The donor must revoke before burial.

Key Takeaway
The donor's right to revoke a gift is a personal option that ends with the death of either party after delivery, and is not inherited.

Explicit Compensation Bars Gift Revocation
Explains that compensation given explicitly as such prevents revocation of gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of explicit compensation on gift revocation and identify the conditions required for valid compensation.

Core Idea
Explicit compensation given for a gift nullifies the donor's right to revoke.

In Hanafi law, a donor generally has the right to revoke a gift. However, if the donee gives compensation specifically for the gift, using clear wording, the donor loses that right. This lesson explores the hadith-based ruling and the conditions that make compensation effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that compensation (ʿiwaḍ) added to the gift prevents revocation. Understand that this is a barrier (maniʿ) to the donor's right to take back the gift.
2
Locate the dalil
The ruling is based on the hadith: 'unless he gives compensation for it' (ما لم يثب عنها). This is a narrated proof with zanni transmission and indication.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'unless he gives compensation' indicates that compensation removes the donor's right to revoke. The reasoning is that the right to revoke exists because of a defect in the purpose of the gift, and compensation removes that defect, like a defect in a sale that is later cured.
4
Identify the conditions for compensation
The text lists key conditions: compensation must be explicitly linked to the gift (e.g., 'take this as compensation for your gift'), the donee must take possession (qabd) and segregate it, and it must not come from the gifted property itself.
5
Note the nature of compensation
Compensation in gift is not a true commutative exchange but a donation with the effect of cutting off revocation. Therefore, no riba concerns arise, and the compensation can be of any amount or type.

Source Text

His statement: 'and compensation added to it' — meaning to the gift, and it being a barrier to revocation due to the hadith mentioned earlier: 'unless he gives compensation for it' and because the right of revocation in gift is due to a defect in its purpose, and that has been removed, so it becomes like the case when a buyer finds a defect in the purchased item and then the defect is removed. And it is necessary that he mentions a phrase by which the donor knows that it is compensation for his gift, such as saying: 'Take this as compensation for your gift' or 'as recompense for your gift' or 'in exchange for your gift'. The author indicated this by saying 'as in saying take this as compensation for your gift'. This specific wording is required because the donor has a right of revocation, and it is not dropped by compensation he does not consent to. So he must be informed that it is compensation for his gift. And the compensation is qualified by being added to the gift; because if he does not add it, such as simply giving a gift without saying 'this is in compensation for your gift', it is a separate gift, not compensation, and each has the right to revoke. In al-Mabsut: whether the compensation is little or much, of the same type as the gift or different, because this is not a pure commutative contract so no riba arises. The effect of compensation is only to cut off the right of revocation to achieve the purpose. It is mentioned in al-Kifayah and others. Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage explains that when the donee gives compensation ('iwad) for the gift, using explicit wording that indicates it is in return for the gift, the donor loses the right to revoke. The justification is based on the hadith 'unless he gives compensation for it' and the principle that the right to revoke exists only because of a defect in the purpose of the gift; once compensation is received, that defect is removed. The compensation must be explicitly stated as such; otherwise it is considered a separate gift. The amount or type of compensation does not matter, and no riba concerns apply because it is not a commutative contract. Conditions for the compensation include delivery and segregation, and it must not be from the gifted property itself. The passage is from the commentary on the matn and refers to al-Mabsut and al-Kifayah.

Key Terms
عوض (ʿiwaḍ): Compensation or return given by the donee to the donor for the gift, intended to prevent revocation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Give compensation before delivery of the gift.

Key Takeaway
Explicit compensation for a gift, with delivery, bars the donor's right to revoke.

Effect of Compensation on Revocation of Gift
Conditions for compensation to prevent gift revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which compensation prevents revocation of a gift and apply the Hanafi position to scenarios involving compensation from the gifted property.

Core Idea
Compensation prevents revocation only if explicitly stated; compensation from the gifted property does not suffice.

In this passage, we examine how compensation given by the donee (or a third party) can block the donor's right to revoke a gift. The text establishes that the compensation must be explicitly designated as such and must come from outside the gifted property. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar regarding partial compensation from the gifted item itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal effect of compensation
Recognize that compensation (ʿiwaḍ) prevents the donor from revoking the gift. This is based on the hadith 'as long as he is not compensated for it' and the analogy of a buyer who loses the right to return a defective item once the defect is removed.
The effect is to make the gift binding (lāzim).
2
Determine the required wording
Compensation must be explicitly stated as such. The donor must know that what he receives is in exchange for the gift. Phrases like 'take this as compensation for your gift' achieve this.
A mere gift from the donee without such wording is a new gift (hibah mubtadaʾah) and does not affect revocation.
3
Identify the conditions of compensation
Like a gift, compensation must be taken into possession (qabd) and segregated. Importantly, it must be from outside the gifted property. A part of the gifted item does not qualify.
Compensation can be less or more than the gift, in kind or different, as it is a gratuity not a sale.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar
Zufar holds that compensation from the gifted property itself is valid because the donee owns it fully after possession. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) reject this, arguing the donor intended a separate compensation.
The majority position is mufta bihi.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether the donor can revoke by checking: (1) Was compensation explicitly stated? (2) Is it from outside the gifted property? (3) Was it possessed by the donor?
If conditions are met, revocation is prohibited.
