Seven Preventers of Gift Revocation in Hanafi Fiqh
Lists seven preventers of gift revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven preventers of gift revocation and distinguish between factors that do and do not prevent revocation according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Revocation is blocked by seven specific changes; not every change bars the right.

This passage from the matn enumerates the factors that prevent a donor from revoking a gift (hiba) once it has been delivered. Understanding these preventers is crucial for determining when a gift becomes irrevocable under Hanafi law. The text first provides a mnemonic list of seven items, then expands on twelve factors from Khizanat al-Fiqh and lists nine situations that do not bar revocation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven preventers from the mnemonic
The text gives a succinct mnemonic: 'فزيادة موصولة موت عوض وخروجها عن ملك موهوب له زوجية قرب هلاك قد عرض'. Break it down word by word: increase that is connected, death, compensation, leaving the donee's ownership, marriage, close blood relationship (mahram), and destruction. List each and understand its meaning.
The mnemonic uses the first letters: ف (faa), ز (zay), م (meem), ع (ayn), خ (kha), ز (zay), ق (qaf), ه (haa).
2
Understand 'connected increase' (ziyadah muttasilah)
A connected increase is one that is physically attached and cannot be separated without damage, e.g., a slave growing up, land being built upon, a garment being dyed or sewn. This is distinct from a mere increase in value or a separable increase like fruit or offspring.
The text later clarifies that mere value increase does not bar revocation.
3
Distinguish factors that prevent from those that do not
After listing the seven, the passage contrasts nine things that do NOT cut off the right to revoke, such as the gifted item giving birth (mother returns without child), tree bearing fruit (tree returns without fruit), or the donee cutting cloth but not sewing it. Note the key difference: connected physical change vs. separable addition or value change.
The nine non-preventers are given to avoid confusion with the seven preventers.
4
Apply to example scenarios
Test your understanding: (a) The donee sells the gift – does the donor still have the right to revoke? (b) The donee builds a house on the gifted land – can the donor take it back? (c) The donee marries the donor – does that block revocation? Answer each using the seven preventers.
Selling means the gift left the donee's ownership, which is a preventer, so no revocation. Building is a connected increase, so no revocation. Marriage is a preventer, so no revocation.
5
Recognize the broader interpretation from Khizanat al-Fiqh
The passage cites Khizanat al-Fiqh, which lists twelve factors that cut off the right to return, including specific formulations of compensation (e.g., 'take this as compensation for tearing'). Understand that the seven preventers are a concise summary, while the twelve provide more detail on how compensation must be phrased to prevent revocation.
The passage concludes: 'Interpretation of the mentioned preventives is possible, so reflect.' This encourages ijtihad within the Hanafi framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منع الرجوع في الهبة سبعة: فزيادة موصولة موت عوض وخروجها عن ملك موهوب له زوجية قرب هلاك قد عرض. والحاصل أن هذه الأشياء السبعة تمنع الرجوع عن الهبة. وقال في «خزانة الفقه»: اثنا عشر شيئاً ينقطع به حق الرجوع إذا كان الموهوب له ذا رحم محرم منه، أو كانت زوجته، أو كان زوجها، أو كان أجنبيا، وعوضها وقال: خذ هذا عوض هتك أو بدلاً عنها، أو جزاء عنها، أو مكافأة عنها، أو في مقابلها، أو مات أحدهما أو خرج عن ملكه. أو زاد فيها زيادة متصلة بأن كان عبداً صغيراً فكبر، أو كان مهزولاً فسمن، أو كان أرضاً فبنى فيها، أو كان ثوباً فخاطه أو صبغه صبغاً يزيد، أو غيره بأن كان حنطة فطحنها، أو دقيقاً فخبزه، أو سويقاً فلته بسمن، أو كان لبناً فاتخذه جبناً أو سمناً، أو كانت جارية فعلمها القرآن والكتابة والمشط. وتسعة أشياء لا ينقطعُ به حق الرجوع: إذا زادت قيمته أو ولدت الموهوبة يرجعُ في الأم دون الولد، أو أثمرت الشجرة يرجعُ في الشجرِ دون الثمر، أو كان ثوباً فقطعه ولم يخطه، أو كان داراً فانهدم شيء منها، أو وهب لبني عمه أو في مرضه لورثته ثم مات الواهب عقبه فلورثته الرجوع، أو وهب لأخيه ولأجنبي عبداً يرجع في نصيب الأجنبي، أو استحق العوض يرجعُ في الهبة، أو استحق الهبة رجع في العوض. انتهى. والتأويل في الموانع المذكورة ممكن فتفكر.
Prevention of returning in the gift is seven: increase that is connected, death, compensation, its leaving from the ownership of the donee, marriage relationship, blood relationship, destruction that has occurred. So these seven things prevent returning the gift. And it was said in Khizanat al-Fiqh: Twelve things cut off the right of return: if the donee is a mahram relative, or his wife, or her husband, or a stranger, and compensation; and he says: take this as compensation for tearing, or as a substitute, or as recompense, or as a reward, or in exchange for it; or one of them dies; or it leaves his ownership; or he adds to it a connected increase, such as the slave was small and grew up, or was skinny and became fat, or was land and he built on it, or was a garment and he sewed it or dyed it with a dye that increases, or other: wheat and he milled it, or flour and he baked it, or ground grain and he mixed it with ghee, or milk and he made it into cheese or butter, or a slave girl and he taught her Quran, writing, and combing. And nine things do not cut off the right of return: if its value increases, or the gifted gives birth—he returns the mother without the child; or the tree bears fruit—he returns the tree without the fruit; or it was a garment and he cut it but did not sew it; or it was a house and part of it collapsed; or he gave to his cousins or in his illness to his heirs, then the donor died afterwards—his heirs have the right to return; or he gave to his brother and a stranger a slave—he returns the stranger's share; or the compensation is invalidated—he returns the gift; or the gift is invalidated—he returns the compensation. End. Interpretation of the mentioned preventives is possible, so reflect.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage from the matn enumerates the factors that prevent the donor from revoking a gift (hiba) once it has been delivered. The text first provides a mnemonic list of seven items: (1) a connected increase (e.g., the slave grew up, land was built upon), (2) death of either party, (3) compensation ('iwad) given by the donee, (4) the gifted item leaving the donee's ownership (e.g., resold), (5) marriage between donor and donee, (6) close blood relationship (mahram), (7) destruction of the gifted item. Then, citing Khizanat al-Fiqh, it expands to twelve factors that definitively end the right to revoke, including specific situations where compensation is phrased as 'for tearing' or 'substitute' etc. It also lists nine situations that do NOT bar revocation, such as mere increase in value, birth of offspring (donor can take back the mother but not the child), fruit of a tree, or partial cutting of cloth without sewing. The passage concludes that these preventives can be interpreted broadly and urges the reader to reflect.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A gift; a contract transferring ownership without compensation
رجوع في الهبة (rujūʿ fī al-hibah): The donor's legal right to revoke the gift and reclaim the property
عوض (ʿiwaḍ): Compensation given by the donee to the donor, often in exchange for the gift
زيادة متصلة (ziyādah muttaṣilah): A connected increase that becomes part of the gift and cannot be removed without damage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following does NOT prevent the donor from revoking the gift?
A) The donee sells the gifted item
B) The donor marries the donee
C) The gifted slave gives birth to a child
D) The gifted land is built upon

Key Takeaway
A donor loses the right to revoke a gift when one of seven specific preventers occurs, most notably a connected increase to the gift, but separable additions like birth or fruit do not block revocation.

Return of Gift When Half the Compensation Is Invalidated: Abu Hanifa vs. Zufar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial invalidation of gift compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the return of a gift when half the compensation is invalidated, contrast it with Zufar's view, and apply the option (khiyār) to real cases.

Core Idea
When half the compensation is invalidated, the donor cannot reclaim the gift until the remaining compensation is returned.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses a nuanced situation: a gift was given with compensation, and later half of that compensation is proven to belong to someone else (istiḥqāq). The key legal question is whether the donor can immediately reclaim part of the gift. The Hanafi master Abu Hanifa and his student Zufar differ, each using distinct legal reasoning. Let's unpack their arguments and see the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two invalidation scenarios
The passage distinguishes between invalidation of half the gift and invalidation of half the compensation. First, state the ruling for each scenario according to Abu Hanifa.
When half the gift is invalidated, the donor returns half the compensation. When half the compensation is invalidated, he cannot reclaim anything until he returns the remainder.
2
Contrast with Zufar's view
Zufar holds that if half the compensation is invalidated, the donor can immediately reclaim half the gifted item. Understand his analogy to a sale.
Zufar draws qiyas: each part is a counterpart; just as in a sale, if half the price is invalidated, the seller can reclaim half the goods.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response relies on the principle that the remaining compensation suffices as full compensation at the outset (istihsan). Since continuation is easier than initiation, the donor has an option, not an automatic right.
The donor may either return the remaining compensation and reclaim the gift, or keep it and forfeit his right.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under Zufar's view, the donor could reclaim half the gift immediately without returning the remainder. Under the preferred view, he must return first or keep all.
The option preserves fairness: the donor cannot benefit from partial compensation while keeping part of the gift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورجع في استحقاق نصف الهبة بنصف عوضها. لا في استحقاق نصف العوض حتى يَرُدَّ ما بقي. هذا عندنا، وعند زُفَرَ يرجع بالنصف اعتباراً بالعوض الآخر. ولنا: أنه ظَهَرَ بالاستحقاق أنَّ العوض هو الباقي فقط، فما لم يرده لا يرجع بالهبة، وإنما يكون له حق الرَّد؛ لأنه لم يُسقط حق الرجوع إلا أن يسلّم له كل العوض، ولم يسلم. ولو عوض عن نصف الهبة يرجع بنصف العوض كما في البيع. قوله: لا في...الخ؛ يعني لا يرجعُ الواهب إذا استحق نصف العوض بشيء، وحتى يردّ باقيه. قوله: وعند زفـر...الخ؛ يعني إن زفـر له ذهب إلى أن الواهب يرجعُ بنصف الموهوب اعتباراً بالعوض الآخر، وهو الموهوب، فإنه لو استحق نصفه يرجعُ بنصف العوض؛ لأن كلَّ واحدٍ منهما يصير مقابلاً بالآخر، كما في بيع العرض، فإنه إذا استحق نصف أحدهما يكون للمستحق عليه أن يرجع على صاحبه بنصف ما يقابله. قوله: ولنا...الخ؛ تقريره: إن الباقـي مـن الـعـوض يـصـلـح عـوضـاً للكـل في الابتداء، وما يصلح عوضاً عن الكلِّ في الابتداء يصح أن يكون عوضاً عنه في البقاء؛ لأنَّ البقاء أسهل من الابتداء. وباستحقاق نصف نصف العوض ظهرَ أنَّ العوض هو الباقي فقط، إلا أنه يتخير بين أن يرد ما بقي من العوض ويرجع في الهبة وبين أن يمسكه ولا يرجع بشيء؛ لأنَّ الواهب لم يسقط حقه في الرجوع إلا ليسلّم له كلّ العوض، ولم يسلّم له، فله أن يرد ما بقي من العوض. قوله: ولو عوض... الخ؛ أي ولو عوّض عن نصف الهبة فللواهب أن يرجع بما لم تعوض؛ لأن المانع قد خص النصف، غاية ما فيه أنه يلزم منه الشيوع في الهبة، لكنه صار فلا يضره. وذكر صاحب «المنح» نقلاً عن «المجتبى»: إنَّ العوض المانع من الرجوع هـو المشروط في عقدِ الهبة، أما إذا عوّضه بعده فلا، ولم أرَ مَن صرَّح به غيره. انتهى.
And he returns in the invalidation of half the gift by half of its compensation. Not in the invalidation of half the compensation until he returns what remains. This is according to us, and according to Zufar he returns by half considering the other compensation. And for us: it became clear by the invalidation that the compensation is only the remainder, so as long as he does not return it, he does not return the gift, and he only has the right to reject; because he did not revoke the right of return except that he would be handed over all the compensation, and it was not handed over. And if he compensates for half the gift, he returns half the compensation as in sale. His saying: "Not in..." means: the donor does not return anything if half the compensation is invalidated, until he returns the rest. His saying: "And according to Zufar..." means: Zufar went to the view that the donor returns half the gifted item considering the other compensation, which is the gifted item; for if half of it is invalidated, he returns half the compensation; because each of them becomes a counterpart of the other, as in the sale of goods, if half of one is invalidated, the one entitled can reclaim half of its counterpart. His saying: "And for us..." its explanation: The remainder of the compensation can serve as compensation for the whole at the beginning, and what can serve as compensation for the whole at the beginning is valid as compensation for it in continuation; because continuation is easier than commencement. And by the invalidation of half the compensation, it became clear that the compensation is only the remainder, except that he has the option between returning what remains of the compensation and taking back the gift, or keeping it and not taking back anything; because the donor did not drop his right to return except that he would be given all the compensation, and it was not given, so he may return what remains of the compensation. His saying: "And if he compensates..." means: if he compensates for half the gift, then the donor may take back what was not compensated; because the preventer has been specified for the half; the utmost is that it entails co-ownership in the gift, but it has become so and does not harm him. And the author of al-Manh mentioned quoting al-Mujtaba: The compensation that prevents return is the one stipulated in the gift contract; as for if he compensates after it, then it does not prevent; and I have not seen anyone else state this. End.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (commentary) expands on a ruling from the matn regarding the return of a gift when half of it is invalidated. The ruling: if half the gift is invalidated (e.g., it turns out not to belong to the donor), the donor may reclaim half the compensation he gave. However, if half the compensation is invalidated, the donor cannot reclaim anything until he returns the remaining compensation. This is the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa). Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl disagreed: he held that the donor can immediately reclaim half the gifted item, drawing an analogy to a sale where each part is a counterpart of the other. The Hanafi response relies on the principle that the remaining compensation suffices as full compensation at the outset; since the donor only gave it expecting full return, he has the option to either keep it (and forfeit his right) or return it and take back the gift. The commentary also notes that if the donor compensated only half of the gift (i.e., partial compensation), he may retract what was not compensated. Additionally, a nuance from al-Mujtaba: only compensation stipulated in the contract bars return; post-contract compensation does not. This is a detailed intra-Hanafi legal analysis.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Invalidation of ownership; a third party proves rightful ownership, nullifying the gift or compensation.
عوض (ʿiwaḍ): Compensation given by the donee in exchange for the gift, often monetary or in kind.
خيار (khiyār): Option given to the donor to either return the remaining compensation and reclaim the gift, or keep it and lose the right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa), what must the donor do if half the compensation for a gifted item is invalidated?
A) Immediately reclaim half the gifted item
B) Return the remaining compensation and then reclaim the entire gift
C) Keep the remaining compensation and forfeit the right to reclaim
D) Both B and C are valid options

Key Takeaway
When half the compensation for a gift is invalidated, the donor has an option: return the remainder and reclaim the gift, or keep it and lose the right.

Revocation of Gift When Half the Compensation Is Due: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
States Hanafi ruling on revocation when half of compensation is due, with Zufar's intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revocation when half of the compensation is realized, explain Zufar's opposing view and its basis in qiyas, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to determine the donor's options.

Core Idea
A donor cannot revoke a gift unless he returns the defective compensation; continuation is easier than initiation.

This lesson examines a ruling from the 'Chapter on Revocation of Gifts' in the Hanafi tradition. The matn states that when half of the compensation (iwad) given in exchange for a gift is claimed by a third party (istihqaq), the donor may not revoke the gift until he returns the remaining compensation. Imam Zufar, a prominent Hanafi, disagrees, allowing immediate revocation of half. The commentary reveals the legal reasoning—a debate between qiyas and istihsan—and clarifies the operative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn ruling
Read the first line of the matn: 'لا في استحقاق نصف العوض حتى يَرُدُّ ما بقي'. State the rule: when half the compensation is claimed by a third party, the donor cannot revoke until he returns what remains of the compensation.
This is the authoritative Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah).
2
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that the donor may revoke half of the gift immediately, without returning anything. His analogy: in a sale, if half of the price is claimed, the seller can reclaim half of the goods. Here, the gift and compensation are counterparts, so the same rule applies.
Zufar's argument is based on qiyas (analogical reasoning).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (istihsan) is that the remaining compensation suffices for the whole gift at the inception. Since 'continuance is easier than initiation', it remains sufficient. Moreover, the donor only dropped his right of revocation on condition of receiving full compensation, which did not happen.
This shows the use of istihsan to override qiyas.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
According to Abu Hanifa, the donor has two options: either return the remaining compensation and revoke the gift, or keep the remaining compensation and forfeit revocation. According to Zufar, the donor may revoke half immediately without returning anything.
The practical difference (thamarah) is clear: the donor's rights depend on which view is followed.
5
Consider the case of compensating half the gift
The text also addresses a scenario where the donor compensates for half the gift. In that case, the donor may revoke the uncompensated portion because the impediment (the compensation) is specific to that half.
This is consistent with the principle that only the part with compensation prevents revocation.

The Book of Gift, Chapter on Revocation. No revocation when half of the compensation is realized until he returns what remains, and if he compensates... This is our view. According to Zufar: He revokes half, considering the other compensation. And our proof: It has become clear through realization that the compensation is only what remains, so as long as he does not return it, he does not revoke the gift, and he only has the right of refusal because he did not drop his right of revocation except upon the condition that all the compensation be handed over to him, and it was not. [1] His statement: 'No in... etc.' means the donor does not revoke anything when half of the compensation is realized, and until he returns its remainder. [1] His statement: 'According to Zufar... etc.' means that Zufar went to that the donor revokes half of the gift considering the other compensation, which is the gift. So if half of it is realized, he revokes half of the compensation because each of them becomes a counterpart of the other, as in the sale of a commodity: if half of one of them is realized, the one entitled may reclaim from his counterpart half of its equivalency. His statement: 'And our proof... etc.' Its determination: The remaining compensation suffices for the whole initially, and what suffices as compensation for the whole initially is valid as compensation for it in continuance, because continuance is easier than initiation. By the realization of half of the compensation, it becomes clear that the compensation is only what remains, but he has the choice between returning what remains of the compensation and revoking the gift, or keeping it and not revoking anything because the donor did not drop his right of revocation except upon the condition that all the compensation be handed over to him, and it was not handed over, so he may return what remains of the compensation. [4] His statement: 'And if he compensates... etc.' i.e., if he compensates for half of the gift, the donor may revoke what was not compensated because the impediment is specific to half. Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on the matn of al-Wiqaya, addresses the ruling on revocation of a gift (hibah) when half of the compensation (iwad) given in exchange is realized (istihqaq) as belonging to someone else. The matn states that the donor cannot revoke until he returns the remaining compensation. The commentator explains that this is the Hanafi position. A dispute with Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl (also a Hanafi) is presented: Zufar analogizes to sale and allows revocation of half immediately. The Hanafi proof (istihsan) is that the remaining compensation was sufficient for the whole at the inception, and continuance is easier than initiation; thus the donor must either return the remainder and revoke, or keep it and forfeit revocation. The commentary then addresses the case where the donor compensates for half of the gift: he may revoke the uncompensated portion. A quote from al-Munjah specifies that only compensation stipulated in the contract prevents revocation. The unit concludes with the scenario where half of the gift is due: the donor revokes the uncompensated part.

Key Terms
عوض (iwad): The compensation given in exchange for the gift, which may be monetary or otherwise.
رجوع (ruju'): The act of the donor reclaiming the gifted property from the donee.
استحقاق (istihqaq): The situation where a third party proves rightful ownership of part or all of the compensation, making it defective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when half of the compensation is realized as belonging to another, what must the donor do before revoking the gift?
A) Revoke half of the gift immediately.
B) Return the remaining compensation to the donee.
C) Seek the judge's permission.
D) Forfeit the right of revocation entirely.

Key Takeaway
The reliance on istihsan over qiyas means that the donor must return the remaining compensation before revocation, because continuance is easier than initiation.

Revocation of Gift: Partial Sale and Consent Requirement
Explains revocation rules for partial sale and consent requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on revocation when the donee sells half the gift and explain why revocation requires mutual consent or a judge's decree.

Core Idea
Revocation of a gift requires the donee's consent or a judge's decree; donee's sale does not bar revocation of the unsold portion.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies two interconnected issues in gift revocation: how a donee's partial sale affects the donor's right, and the procedural conditions for valid revocation. The commentator emphasizes that the donee's established ownership prevents unilateral revocation, drawing an analogy to the return of a defective item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note that the passage addresses two cases: when the donee sells half the gift and when he sells nothing. In both, the donor may revoke the unsold portion.
The ruling is the same: the donor can revoke the remaining half.
2
Understand the reasoning for partial revocation
The donor has the right to revoke the whole gift. Since compensation only blocks revocation of the sold part, the unsold part remains revocable. This is a proportional application of the right.
The impediment (compensation) is limited to the sold share.
3
Analyze the condition for validity
Revocation is not valid unless done by mutual consent (taradi) between donor and donee or by a judge's decree. This protects the donee's ownership.
A judge has general authority to terminate ownership without consent.
4
Grasp the legal nature of revocation
Revocation is rescission (faskh) of the gift contract. It is not a new transfer, so it requires mutual consent or judicial authority, analogous to returning a defective item after possession.
The analogy highlights that established ownership cannot be undone unilaterally.

For if the purpose was reward, it has been obtained, and if worldly compensation, it has not been obtained. So if there is doubt, a decision is inevitable by consent or decree. Revocation is rescission of a contract, so it is not valid except from one who has general authority, which is the judge, or from both of them due to their authority over themselves, like return due to defect after possession. So if the judge does not decree and they do not mutually rescind, the donee's ownership over the property remains effective until his disposition runs in it, such as freeing, selling, etc., even if after litigation before the judge.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the chapter of revocation in gifts. It first addresses the scenario where the donee sells half of the gifted property. The ruling is that the donor may revoke the remaining half, whether or not the donee sold anything, because the donor's right to revoke the whole is not defeated by the donee's partial sale. The impediment (the compensation) only applies to the sold half. The commentary then emphasizes a crucial procedural rule: revocation of a gift is not valid except by mutual consent (taradi) between donor and donee, or by a decree of a judge (qadi). The reason provided is that the donee's ownership is firmly established in the gifted property; it cannot be removed without consent or judicial authority. The commentator notes that revocation is inherently weak (za'if), as it is contrary to analogy (khilaf al-qiyas) because it involves taking property from another's ownership. The analogy is drawn to the return of a defective item after possession. The passage concludes by affirming that the donee's ownership persists until the judge rules or both parties agree, and the donee may validly transact (e.g., sell, free slaves) even after the donor has initiated litigation.

Key Terms
تراض (taradi): Mutual consent between donor and donee to revoke the gift.
قاض (qadi): A judge with general authority (wilaya 'amma) who can issue a decree to revoke the gift if the donee does not consent.
فسخ العقد (faskh al-'aqd): Rescission or annulment of the contract of gift, which is the legal nature of revocation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Unilaterally by the donor at any time
B) By mutual consent or a judge's decree
C) Only if the gift has not been consumed
D) Only if the donee is not a relative

Key Takeaway
Revocation of a gift requires the donee's consent or a judge's decree, and the donee's sale of part does not prevent revocation of the unsold remainder.

Revocation of Gift When Donee Sells Part
Explains the ruling on revocation after partial sale by the donee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a donor may revoke a gift after partial sale by the donee and apply the rule to a new scenario.

Core Idea
Donor can revoke half the gift if donee sells the other half, but only with mutual consent or court order.

Gift revocation (ruju' fi al-hibah) is a unique right in Hanafi fiqh, permissible but restricted. This lesson explores a specific scenario: the donee sells half the gifted property. The donor may then revoke the remaining half, but the revocation itself requires either mutual consent or a judicial decree. The commentator explains the reasoning and the legal nature of revocation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that this passage discusses revocation of a gift (hibah) when the donee has sold part of it. The donor has a right to revoke the remaining portion.
Revocation is a right, not an obligation; it is permissible (mubah) under conditions.
2
Analyze the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (a) donee sells half, (b) donee sells nothing. In both, the donor may revoke half the gift. Explain that the donor's right over the whole implies the right over part.
The impediment (compensation) is only in the sold portion, so revocation of the unsold part is allowed.
3
Identify the condition for valid revocation
Revocation is not valid except by mutual consent (taradin) or by a judge's decree (qada'). The donee's ownership is established; it can only be removed by consent or court order.
The commentator compares revocation to returning a defective item (khiyar al-'ayb) after possession.
4
Understand the legal nature of revocation
Revocation is a rescission (faskh) of the original gift contract, not a new contract. Hence, it requires general authority (judge) or mutual authority of both parties.
This explains why consent or court order is necessary.

Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn regarding revocation of a gift. It explains two main rules: (1) If the donee sells half of the gifted property, the donor can revoke the remaining half, just as he could revoke the whole if nothing was sold. The commentator reasons that since the donor has the right to revoke the whole, he certainly can revoke a part. (2) Revocation is only valid with mutual consent or a judicial decree. This is because the donee's ownership is established, so it can only be removed by consent or court order. The commentator notes that revocation is a rescission (faskh) of the contract, not a new contract, and hence requires general authority (like a judge) or mutual agreement. The passage also references al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya for further details.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A gift; a contract transferring ownership without compensation.
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation of a gift by the donor, reclaiming the gifted property.
تراض (taradin): Mutual consent of both parties to revoke the gift.
قضاء (qada'): Judicial decree by a judge ordering revocation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a donor may revoke an unsold portion of a gift after partial sale by the donee, but the revocation itself requires mutual consent or a judicial decree.

Revocation of Gift: Rescission vs New Gift – The Zufar Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of gift revocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats revocation of a gift as rescission (faskh) and evaluate Imam Zufar's counter-argument based on qiyas with return for defect.

Core Idea
Revocation of a gift is rescission, not a new gift – the donor exercises a pre-existing right.

In Hanafi fiqh, the revocation of a gift raises a key question: does it create a new transfer or simply undo the original? Imam Zufar argued that revocation by mutual consent is a new gift, analogous to the return of defective goods. The Hanafi majority holds it is a rescission of the original contract. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the refutation of Zufar's analogy, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Determine exactly what is being debated: the nature of revocation when both donor and donee agree to revoke the gift.
The dispute only applies to revocation by mutual consent, not to judicial decree.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi school holds that revocation is a rescission (faskh) of the original gift. The donor had a pre-existing right to rescind because the gift contract was not binding (ghayr lazim). This is analogous to reclaiming a loan (ariyah).
Revocation does not require a new possession (qabd) by the donor and is valid even in an undivided share.
3
Analyze Zufar's counter-argument
Zufar compares revocation by consent to the return of defective goods (radd bi al-ʿayb) in sale, which creates a new contract. He argues that since ownership returns by mutual consent, it should be treated as a fresh gift, requiring possession and counting from the one-third in death illness.
This is a qiyas (analogy) based on the similarity of mutual consent leading to return of property.
4
Examine the Hanafi refutation
The Hanafi response distinguishes the two cases: in return for defect, the buyer's right is only to soundness, not rescission; thus a new contract is necessary. In revocation, the donor has an inherent right to rescind, so no new contract arises. The analogy fails because the underlying legal basis differs.
The passages notes that if the defect is removed, the buyer cannot return the item, proving his right is to soundness only.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if revocation occurs during the donor's death illness, the Hanafi view does not count the returned property from the one-third (since it is a rescission, not a new gift), while Zufar's view would count it from the one-third. This affects the heirs' shares.
This difference is important in inheritance and will calculations during marad al-mawt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وهو مع أحدهما ) : أي الرجوعُ مع التراضي، أو قضاء القاضي [1] قوله : وهو مع أحدهما ... الخ ؛ يعني أن المرجوع إذا كان بتراضيهما أو بقضاء القاضي يكون فسخاً من الأصل لا هبة للواهب، وقال زفر : الرجوع بالتراضي عقد جديد، فيجعلُ بمنزلة الهبة المتبدأة ؛ لأنَّ الملك عاد إليه بتراضيهما ، فأشبه الردّ بالعيب ؛ ولهذا لو ردّه في مرض موته يعتبر من الثلث. ولنا : إنّ عقد الهبة انعقد موجباً حق الفسخ للواهب، وهو بالفسخ يكون مستوفياً حقاً ثابتاً له بالعقد ؛ لأنّ العقد وقع غير لازم، فإذا رفع رجع إليه عين ملكه كالعارية، فيكون فسخاً في حق الكلّ، فلا يمكن أن يجعل هبة مبتدأة. ولهذا لا يشترط فيه قبض الواهب، ويصح في الشائع بخلاف الرد بالعيب بعد القبض ؛ لأن حقه فيه وصف السلامة لا في الفسخ ؛ ولهذا لو زال العيب امتنع الردّ، لوصول حقه إليه، لكن إذا لم يكن سليما فات رضاه، فيرجع بالعوض، ويلزم منه فسخ العقد ضرورة من غير أن يثبت حقه في الفسخ. فإذا لم يكن له حق في الفسخ لم يصر مستوفياً حقه، فيكون ملكاً مبتدأ ضرورة، غير أنه إذا حكم القاضي بالردّ عند عجزه عن التسليم حقه جعلناه فسخاً لعموم ولايته، ولا كذلك المتعاقدان ؛ لأنه لا دية لهما إلا على أنفسهما. وإنما اعتبر ردّه في المرض من الثلث ؛ لأنَّ حق الورثة تعلّق بجميع ماله ، فلا يقدر أن يبطله باختياره ، وإن أبطله ردَّ عليه كيف ما كان استحساناً، وفي القياس أن يردّ ، (1) ذكره ابن سماعة . كذا في «التبيين»، وغيره. (۱) تبيين الحقائق (۵ : ۱۰۱ - ۱۰۲).
Before the judicial decree, it is valid; and if he prevents him and it perishes, he does not guarantee; and it is with one of them. (Before the judicial decree, it is valid): i.e., the donee emancipates the gifted property. (And if he prevents him and it perishes, he does not guarantee): i.e., the donee prevents the gifted property from the donor after he revoked, but the judge did not rule, so it perishes in the donee's hand; he does not guarantee. [1] His statement: 'And it is with one of them...' means that revocation, if by mutual consent or judicial decree, is a rescission from the origin, not a gift for the donor. Zufar said: Revocation by mutual consent is a new contract, so it is treated like a fresh gift, because ownership returned to him by their mutual consent, so it resembles the return for a defect. Hence, if he returns it in his death illness, it is considered from the one-third. Our position: The gift contract was concluded giving the donor a right of rescission, and by rescinding, he is exercising a right established for him by the contract, because the contract was not binding. So when he lifts it, his property returns to him like a loan (ariyah), so it is a rescission regarding the whole, thus it cannot be made a fresh gift. For this reason, possession (qabd) by the donor is not required, and it is valid in an undivided share (shai'), unlike the return for a defect after possession, because the right there is the description of soundness, not rescission. Hence, if the defect is removed, the return is prevented because his right has been fulfilled. But if it is not sound, his consent is absent, so he returns for compensation, necessitating rescission of the contract out of necessity without his having a right of rescission. Since he has no right of rescission, he is not exercising his right, so it is a new ownership out of necessity. However, if the judge rules for return when unable to deliver his right, we make it a rescission due to his general authority, not so for the two contracting parties, because they have no authority except over themselves. The reason it is considered from the one-third in his death illness is that the heirs' right attaches to all his property, so he cannot nullify it by his choice; if he nullifies it, it is returned to him as a matter of istihsan; by analogy it would be returned. (1) Mentioned by Ibn Sama'a. Similarly in al-Tabyin and others. (1) Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (5:101-102).
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal nature of revocation of a gift. It first states that if the donee emancipates the gifted slave before a court decision, it is valid. If the donee prevents the donor from taking possession and the property perishes, the donee is not liable, because his hand is not considered a guarantee (yad dhaman) unless he prevents after a demand and is able to deliver. The main dispute is between the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa) and Imam Zufar. Zufar holds that revocation by mutual consent is a new gift (hibah musta'nafah), like the return of defective goods (radd bi al-'ayb). Consequently, if done during a death illness, it is counted from the one-third of the estate. The Hanafi position, however, is that revocation is a rescission (faskh) of the original contract, not a new gift. This is because the gift contract gave the donor a right to rescind, as it was not binding (ghayr lazim). By rescinding, the donor reclaims his property, similar to a loan (ariyah). Therefore, possession (qabd) by the donor is not required, and revocation is valid even in an undivided share (shai'), unlike the return for defect. The text differentiates between the two cases: in the return for defect, the buyer's right is only to soundness, not rescission; thus, if the defect is removed, return is prevented. In revocation, the donor has an inherent right to rescind. The passage concludes by noting that the judge's decree can make return a rescission due to his general authority, but parties themselves cannot create a rescission by consent except as a rescission of the original contract. The citation from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq supports this.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, annulling it from the beginning.
عقد جديد (aqd jadid): A new contract; Zufar's view that revocation creates a new gift.
رد بالعيب (radd bi al-'ayb): Return of a defective item by the buyer to reclaim the price.
عارية (ariyah): Loan; used as an analogy for revocation being a return of property without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a donor revokes a gift by mutual consent, is this considered a rescission of the original gift or a new gift?
A) A new gift, because the parties agreed
B) A rescission of the original gift
C) Both a rescission and a new gift simultaneously
D) Neither; it is a nullity

Key Takeaway
Revocation of a gift by mutual consent is a rescission (faskh) of the original contract, not a new gift; this has practical consequences for possession and death illness calculations.

Donee's Manumission and Liability Before Judicial Ruling on Gift Revocation
States two rulings on donee's actions and liability before judicial ruling on gift revocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the validity of a donee's manumission before a judicial ruling on gift revocation and explain when the donee is liable for the gifted object's destruction.

Core Idea
Before the judge rules, the donee's manumission is valid and his possession is non-liable unless he refuses delivery when able.

In Hanafi law, a donor may revoke a gift, but the revocation is not automatically effective; it requires a judge's ruling. This lesson examines two key rulings: the validity of the donee's actions (like manumission) before that ruling, and the circumstances under which the donee becomes liable for the object if it perishes while in his possession. Understanding these rulings clarifies the legal nature of possession during the revocation period.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two rulings
Separate the passage into two distinct legal statements: the validity of the donee's manumission before qada, and the donee's non-liability for destruction.
2
Analyze the condition for liability
Identify the exception that triggers liability: the donor must demand return, and the donee must be able to deliver but refuse.
The exception is introduced by 'إلا إذا'.
3
Relate to possession types
Understand why the donee's hand is generally non-liable (yad ghayr madmuna) and how the exception creates a transition to liability.
Possession of a gift is permissive until final revocation.
4
Apply to sample cases
Test scenarios: donee prevents but does not refuse demand; donee refuses but is unable; donee refuses when able. Determine liability in each.
The key is the concurrence of demand, ability, and refusal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Gift, Chapter on Revocation in Gifts: Before the judge's ruling it is valid. And if he prevents [the return], and it perishes, he does not guarantee. (Before the judge's ruling it is valid): meaning the donee manumitted the gifted [object]. (And if he prevents, and it perishes, he does not guarantee): meaning the donee prevented the donor from [taking] the gifted [object] after the latter rescinded but the judge had not ruled, so the gifted [object] perished in the hands of the donee; he does not guarantee. Likewise if it perishes in his hands after the judge's ruling; because his hand is not liable, except if [the donor] requested it and he prevented [return] despite being able to deliver.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains two rulings from the matn of al-Hidayah on revocation of gifts. First: if the donee manumits the gifted object before the judge issues a ruling on the revocation, the manumission is valid. Second: if the donor has revoked the gift (without a judge's ruling yet) and requests return, but the donee prevents it, and then the object is destroyed in the donee's hands, the donee is not liable for its value. This is because the donee's possession, though not rightful after revocation, is not a guaranteed possession (yad ghayr madmuna) unless he refuses to deliver when able. The ruling holds even after a judge's ruling on revocation, as long as the donee is not obstructive. The exception is when the donor demands return and the donee is able but refuses—then he becomes liable.

Key Terms
قبل القضاء (qabl al-qada'): Before the judge issues a ruling on the revocation of the gift.
يد غير مضمونة (yad ghayr madmuna): A hand (possession) that is not liable for compensation if the object perishes, i.e., the possessor is not a guarantor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Before a judicial ruling on gift revocation, the donee's actions are valid and his possession is non-liable, except when he refuses delivery after a demand when able.

Revocation as Rescission vs. New Gift: Dispute with Zufar
Explains that revocation is a rescission, not a new gift, and responds to Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats gift revocation as a rescission and not a new gift, and distinguish it from return due to defect.

Core Idea
A gift revocation exercises a pre-existing right; it is a rescission, not a new gift.

This lesson examines the legal nature of gift revocation (ruju'). When a donor and donee agree to revoke a gift, is that contract a mere rescission (faskh) of the original gift, or is it a new gift from the donee back to the donor? Imam Zufar argued the latter, but the majority Hanafi position, held by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, treats it as a rescission. Understanding this distinction has practical consequences for requirements of delivery, validity in joint property, and its treatment in death-illness.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two positions
Identify the core question: Is revocation by mutual consent a rescission (faskh) from the origin or a new gift contract? The Hanafi majority says rescission; Zufar says new gift.
The Hanafi position is that the original gift contract included a right of rescission for the donor because it is non-binding (ghayr lazim).
2
Examine Zufar's proof (qiyas)
Zufar analogizes revocation to the return of a defective item (radd bil-'ayb), where ownership returns by mutual consent, making it a new transfer. Understand the analogical reasoning.
Zufar's analogy: just as in radd bil-'ayb the buyer returns the item and ownership transfers anew, here the donee returns the gift, so it should be a new gift.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response and refutation
The Hanafi response (lana) points out that in radd bil-'ayb, the buyer's right is to soundness (salamah), not rescission. In revocation, the donor has a pre-existing right to rescind. Thus the analogy fails.
The text also notes that if the defect ceases, return is blocked, confirming the right is to soundness, not rescission.
4
Explore the practical differences (thamarah al-khilaf)
If revocation is a new gift (Zufar), it would require the donor to take possession and would be invalid in joint property. If rescission (Hanafi), no delivery needed and valid in joint property.
In death-illness, the Hanafi school still considers it from the third by istihsan to protect heirs, even though it is a rescission.
5
Understand the final tarjih and its basis
The text adopts the rescission view as mufta bihi. The tarjih is based on the inherent right of the donor in the gift contract and the flawed analogy of Zufar.
This is from the zahir al-riwayah and supported by the majority of Hanafi scholars.

(And it is with one of the two): i.e., the revocation is by mutual consent or by the judge's ruling. [1] His saying: 'And it is with one of the two...' etc.; meaning that when the revocation is by their mutual consent or by the judge's ruling, it is a rescission from the origin, not a gift for the donor. Zufar said: Revocation by mutual consent is a new contract, so it is treated as a renewed gift; because ownership returned to him by their mutual consent, so it resembles return due to defect; hence if he returns it in his death-illness, it is considered from the third. Our proof: The gift contract was concluded entitling the donor to a right of rescission, and by rescinding he is exercising a right established for him by the contract; because the contract was not binding, so when he lifts it, the very object of his property returns to him, like a loan (ariyah), so it is a rescission in everyone's regard, and cannot be made a renewed gift. Therefore the donor's taking possession is not required, and it is valid in undivided shares (musha'), unlike return due to defect after taking possession; because his right there is the condition of soundness, not rescission; hence if the defect ceases, return is prevented, as his right is fulfilled. But if it is not sound, his satisfaction is lost, so he returns for compensation, and this necessitates rescission of the contract by necessity without establishing his right to rescission. Since he has no right to rescission, he is not exercising his right, so it is a new ownership necessarily, except that if the judge orders return when he is unable to deliver his right, we make it a rescission due to the generality of his authority, unlike the two contractors; because they have no authority except over themselves. And why his return in illness is considered from the third: because the heirs' right attaches to all his property, so he cannot nullify it by his choice, and if he nullifies it, it is returned upon him however it may be, by istihsan, and by qiyas it would be returned. Rescission from the origin, not a gift for the donor, so his taking possession is not required, and it is valid in joint property [1] His saying: 'Rescission from the origin'; know that their meaning by 'rescission from the origin' is that no effect arises from the contract in the future, not that its effect is nullified entirely with respect to the past; otherwise the proceeds of separate accessories generated [from the object] would revert to the donor's ownership by his revocation, and the buyer's use of the sold object before return when returning due to defect by a judge's ruling would be prohibited, but that is not so. So in Jami' al-Fusulayn, and the detail in Fatawa Qadi Khan, etc. from the expansive works. (2) His saying: 'And it is valid in joint property'; i.e., revocation is valid in joint property that is divisible, as when he gave a gift and wants to revoke half of it; because consideration of possession and prevention of joint property applies only to transfer of ownership, not to return of old ownership.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal nature of revocation (ruju') in gifts. The matn states that revocation, whether by mutual consent or by a judge's ruling, is a rescission (faskh) from the origin, not a new gift. Imam Zufar disagreed, arguing that revocation by mutual consent is a new contract akin to a renewed gift, since ownership returns by mutual agreement, similar to the return of a defective item. The Hanafi response (lana) explains that the gift contract inherently includes a right of rescission for the donor; he is exercising an existing right, not creating a new transfer. Thus revocation does not require the donor to take delivery (qabd), and it is valid even in joint property (musha'), unlike a new gift which would require possession and would be invalid in joint property. The distinction from return due to defect ('ayb) is clarified: in that case, the buyer's right is to soundness, not rescission, so it becomes a new sale if the judge orders return. The commentary also clarifies that 'rescission from the origin' means the contract ceases to have effect going forward, but does not retroactively nullify all effects (e.g., the donor takes back accessory yields generated in the donee's hands). The validity of revocation in joint property is because the prohibition on joint property concerns transfer of new ownership, not the return of old ownership.

Key Terms
فسخ من الأصل (faskh min al-asl): Rescission from the origin: the contract is considered void from the start in terms of future effects, but not retroactively nullified.
الرد بالعيب (al-radd bil-'ayb): Return of a defective item in a sale; it is considered a new transfer of ownership, not a rescission.
المشاع (al-musha'): Joint property or undivided shares; a gift of joint property is invalid without division, but revocation of such is valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In radd, the buyer has a right to rescission; in ruju', the donor does not.
B) In radd, the buyer's right is to soundness, not rescission; in ruju', the donor has a pre-existing right to rescind.
C) Both are rescissions.
D) Both are new transfers.

Key Takeaway
Gift revocation is a rescission exercising an existing right, not a new transfer; it requires no delivery and is valid in joint property.

Gift with Condition of Compensation (Hiba bi-Shart al-'Iwad)
Ruling statement on gift with condition of compensation and donee's non-recourse for perished gift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hiba bi-shart al-'iwad, list its conditions for validity, and explain why the donee cannot seek recourse if the gift perishes.

Core Idea
A gift with condition of compensation remains a gift, requiring mutual delivery and void in joint property; the donee bears the risk.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies two important aspects of Hanafi gift law. First, it explains that a gift is a gratuitous contract—the donee cannot hold the donor liable if the object is later claimed by another. Second, it defines the specific category of 'gift with condition of compensation' (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad) and its special rules, including the requirement of mutual delivery and invalidity in joint property. The commentary also highlights a critical phrasing distinction that determines whether the transaction is a gift or a sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct legal situations: (1) a gift that perishes in the donee's hands and is later claimed by a third party, and (2) a gift made with a condition of compensation.
The analogy with depositary helps clarify the first point.
2
Analyze the perishing case
Determine the ruling: the donee cannot recourse to the donor. Understand why: a gift is tabarru' (gratuitous), so no implied warranty of safety. Contrast with the depositary case where the depositor is liable because the depositor is considered an agent.
3
Define hiba bi-shart al-'iwad
State that it is a unilateral gift at inception, not a sale. The donor gives with the condition that the donee compensates him.
The word 'ala indicates condition; ba indicates exchange.
4
List the conditions
Identify the conditions for validity: (a) delivery of both the gifted object and the compensation, (b) the gifted object must not be joint property (musha'). Explain why each is required.
If either condition is missing, the gift is invalid.
5
Distinguish between 'ala and ba
Understand that using 'ala (condition) keeps it as a gift, while using ba (exchange) makes it a sale from the outset. This affects the legal consequences (e.g., warranty, mutual rights).
This distinction is specific to Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن تلف الموهوب: أي في يد الموهوب له، (فاستحق فضمن الموهوب له لم يرجع على واهيه; لأن الهبة (3) عقد تبرع، فلا يستحق فيها السلامة. (وهي بشرط العوض هبة ابتداءً)، فشرط قبضهما في العوضين، وتبطل بالشيوع): أي يجوز أن يكون: قبضهما; من باب إضافة المصدر إلى الفاعل، والمفعول محذوف للدلالة، ويجوز أن يكون على العكس (3) قوله: لأن الهبة...الخ; تقريره: إن الهبة عقد تبرع لا عقد معاوضة، فلا يستحق فيها السلامة; لعدم لزومها لا صراحة ولا دلالة، أما صراحة فظاهر. وأما دلالة فلان دلالة الالتزام في المعاوضة سلامة البدل له، وهذا المعنى معدوم هاهنا، والموهوب له غير عامل للواهب، بخلاف المودع; فإن الوديعة إذا هلكت في يده واستحقها مستحق وضمنه فإنه يرجع على المودع; لأن المودع عامل له. (1) هذا إذا ذكره بكلمة: على; بأن يقول وهبت هذا العبد لك على أن تعوضني هذا الثوب، وأما إذا ذكره بحرف الباء بأن يقول وهبت لك هذا الثوب بعبدك هذا أو بألف درهم، وقبله الآخر يكون تبعاً ابتداءً وانتهاء. ينظر: «درر الحكام»(2: 244).
Then if the gifted object perishes: i.e., in the hands of the donee, (and it is claimed by another, so the donee becomes liable, he does not have recourse against his donor; because the gift (3) is a gratuitous contract, so one is not entitled to safety in it. (And it is a gift with condition of compensation: it is a unilateral gift at inception), so delivery of both (the object and compensation) is required in the two compensations, and it is invalid in joint property. It is possible that 'delivery of both' means the delivery of the two, or it could be the opposite. (3) His saying: 'because the gift...'; its explanation: The gift is a gratuitous contract, not a commutative contract, so safety is not entitled in it; because it is not binding expressly or implicitly. As for expressly, it is obvious. As for implicitly, because the implicative indication in a commutative contract is the safety of the substitute for him, and this meaning is absent here, and the donee is not an agent for the donor, unlike the depositary; for the deposit if it perishes in his hands and is claimed by a rightful owner and he is made liable, he has recourse against the depositor; because the depositor is his agent. (1) This is when he mentions it with the word 'ala (on the condition that); such as saying: I give you this slave on condition that you compensate me with this garment. But if he mentions it with the letter ba (in exchange for), such as saying: I give you this garment in exchange for your this slave or for a thousand dirhams, and the other accepts, it becomes a sale from beginning to end. See: Durar al-Hukkam (2: 244).
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage explains two separate rulings. First: if the gifted object perishes in the donee's hands and then a third party proves ownership (e.g., the object was stolen), causing the donee to be liable to the true owner, the donee cannot seek recourse from the donor. This is because a gift is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'), so the donee does not have the right to claim safety or freedom from defects, unlike a sale. The donee is not an agent for the donor, unlike a depositary (mudi') who can recover from the depositor because the depositor is considered to have employed him. Second: a gift conditioned on compensation (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad) is considered a unilateral gift at inception, not a sale. Therefore, it requires that both parties deliver (the gifted object and the compensation) – i.e., the donor delivers the object to the donee, and the donee delivers the compensation to the donor. If either fails to deliver, the transaction is not complete. Additionally, it is invalid if the gifted object is joint property (musha'). The commentary clarifies a crucial difference in wording: if the donor uses the word 'ala (upon condition that), it remains a gift; but if he uses the letter ba (in exchange for), it becomes a sale from the outset. This distinction is important for legal classification.

Key Terms
هبة بشرط العوض (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad): A gift conditioned on the donee giving something in return; it is considered a unilateral gift initially, not a sale.
عقد تبرع (aqd tabarru'): A gratuitous contract, where one party benefits the other without receiving compensation in return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a hiba bi-shart al-'iwad (gift conditioned on compensation), what is required for its validity?
A) Only the donor delivers the gift.
B) Both the gift and the compensation must be delivered (qabd).
C) It is valid even if the gift is joint property.
D) The donee has the right to recourse if the gift is defective.

Key Takeaway
Hiba bi-shart al-'iwad is a gift, not a sale, requiring mutual delivery and void in joint property; the donee bears the risk.
